The Necessity of Distinction for the Object of Power
Razi proves that the object of power must be distinct and pre-existing, and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that the object of divine power must be distinct and pre-existing, and how he defends it against the objection of circularity.

Core Idea
The object of power must be distinct to be related to, and distinction implies pre-existence.

Imam Razi presents a rigorous proof that the object of God's power cannot be something indefinite; it must be distinct and already existent prior to the relation of power. He then addresses a potential objection that would seem to undermine this reasoning. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full English translation. Notice the dense, logical style of Imam Razi.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the main claim: the object of power must be distinct. Then trace the reasoning: power is a relation, relation needs distinct relata; hesitation between two things implies distinction.
Look for the words 'تميز' and 'ثابت'.
3
Follow the objection and response
Identify the objection raised and how Razi refutes it. What is the potential circularity? How does he resolve the paradox?
The objection claims that essence is prior but existence is from relation. Razi says essence is determined before, so it cannot be product of power.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final contradiction: if the object is not existent yet distinct, that is a contradiction. So the object must be pre-existent.
This supports the theological doctrine that God's power relates to already existing entities in some sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانى : أن المقدور القادر لا بد وأن يتميز عن غيره ، لأن اقتدار القادر عليه نسبة بين القادر وبينه ومالم يتميز المنسوب إليه عن غيره استحال اختصاصه بتلك النسبة دون غيره ، ولا تمكن القادر من الجمع بين الحركة و السواد بدلا عن الجمع بين السواد والبياض يستدعى إمتياز أحدهما عن الآخر ، ولأن كونه قادراً على إيجاد الحركة بدلا عن السكون ، وبالعكس يستدعى إمتياز كل واحد منهما عن الآخر ، فإن التردد بين الشيئ بن يتوقف على مغايرتهما ، فثبت أنه لا بد من التميز وكل متميز ثابت ، فإذا تعلق القدرة به يتوقف على ثبوته في نفسه ، فلو كان ثبوته لأجل القدرة لزم الدور ولزم إثبات الثابت وأنه محال. . فإن قلت شرط التعلق تحقيق الماهية والحاصل على التعلق هو الوجود . قلت فالذات لما كانت متقررة قبل التعلق لم تكن مقدورة ، لأن إثبات الثابت محال فالمتعلق هو الذي ليس بثابت وهو إما لو جود أو موصوفية الذات بالوجود الكن ذلك محل ، لأنا بينا أن المتعلق متميز والمتميز ثابت ، فإذا ليس بثابت فهو ثابت هذا خلف
Second: the object of power must necessarily be distinct from other things, because the empowerment of the powerful over it is a relation between the powerful and it, and as long as the object of the relation is not distinct from others, it is impossible for that relation to be specific to it to the exclusion of others. And the possibility for the powerful to combine movement and blackness instead of combining blackness and whiteness requires the distinction of one from the other. And because his being able to create movement instead of rest, and vice versa, requires the distinction of each one from the other. For hesitation between two things depends on their being different. So it is established that distinction is necessary, and everything distinct is existent. So if the power is connected to it, it depends on its being existent in itself. If its existence were due to the power, a circularity would follow, and the establishment of what is already established would follow, which is impossible. If you say: the condition of the connection is the realization of the essence, and what results from the connection is existence. I say: the essence, since it is determined before the connection, would not be an object of power, because establishing what is already established is impossible. So the object of connection is that which is not established. It is either existence or the qualification of the essence by existence, but that is a place... For we have shown that the object of connection is distinct, and the distinct is existent. So if it is not existent, it is existent—this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This is a proof that the object of divine power must be distinct and pre-existing. The author argues that power involves a relation between the agent and its object; for the relation to be specific, the object must be distinct from others. Distinction implies existence. Therefore, the object exists prior to the relation of power. If its existence were caused by the power, there would be circularity. An objection is raised: the relation presupposes the essence, and existence results from the relation. The author responds: the essence is determined before the relation, so it cannot be the product of power. Hence the object of power must be something that is not yet existent, but the argument shows that distinct implies existent, leading to a contradiction if the object is not existent. This demonstrates that the object must be pre-existent.

Key Terms
مقدور
تميز
نسبة
ثابت
دور
إثبات الثابت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can relate to completely non-existent objects with no distinction.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the object must be distinct and pre-existent for the relation of power to be meaningful; otherwise, circularity and contradiction arise.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why must the object of power be distinct?
A) Because power cannot relate to two things simultaneously.
B) Because power is a relation and the relation requires distinct relata.
C) Because God cannot create what does not exist.
D) Because the object is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that the object of divine power must be distinct and pre-existent to avoid circularity and contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that the object of God's power must already be distinct and existent in God's knowledge before power acts upon it. Think of a specific area of your life where you feel stuck—a sin you've committed repeatedly or a situation you desperately want to change. Do you treat that situation as something God can only affect after it becomes 'distinct' in your own mind? How might acknowledging that it is already distinct and known to God change the way you supplicate or trust His power to transform it?
?
The objection in the passage suggests that the essence is realized before the act of power. Consider a deep-seated character flaw you've been trying to overcome. Are you assuming that God's power can only change you after you have fully defined the problem? What would it mean for you to live with the certainty that God's power acts on what He already knows—and to surrender your timeline?

The Impossibility of Creating the Already Existing
Razi argues against eternal power creating a temporal effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an eternal power cannot bring a temporal effect into existence without logical contradiction.

Core Idea
An eternal cause cannot produce a temporal effect without contradiction.

Imam Razi presents a concise argument that challenges a common assumption about divine power. The argument hinges on the nature of eternal relations and the logical impossibility of re-creating what already exists. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the compactness of the argument — each phrase carries weight.
The word 'التفلق' may be a typo for 'التعلق' (connection); we will proceed with the given text.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break the argument into three parts: (1) the premise of eternal power, (2) the implication after creation, (3) the conclusion that the eternal relation would cease, which is impossible.
3
Reflect on the key insight
Ask yourself: Why can't an eternal relation cease? How does this prove that eternal power cannot produce a temporal effect?
This connects to the broader Islamic theological debate on the relationship between God's eternal attributes and temporal creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان قادراً من الأول إلى الأبد ثم إذا أوجده لم يبق مقدور الاستحالة إيجاد الموجود وذلك التفلق القديم قد فنى وعدم النديم محال .
Third: if He were powerful from the beginning to eternity, then when He brings it into existence, it would not remain an object of power because it is impossible to bring into existence what already exists. And that eternal connection would have perished, and the perishing of the eternal is impossible.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This is a short proof against the idea that God's power is eternally related to a single effect. If God has eternal power and produces an effect, once the effect exists, the power can no longer be related to it because bringing the existing into existence is impossible. Thus the eternal relation would cease, but the cessation of what is eternal is impossible. Therefore the effect cannot be produced at a later time if the power is eternal.

Key Terms
قديم
مقدور
إيجاد الموجود
تعلق
فناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because God's power is eternal, He can create any temporal effect at any moment simply by willing it, with no further logical constraint.
Correction: Razi argues that if the power is eternally connected to a specific effect, then once the effect is created, the connection would have to end, but an eternal connection cannot end. Thus, a different understanding of how eternal power relates to temporal effects is required — such as the Ash‘ari notion that power relates to the act at the moment of creation, not from eternity in a fixed way.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's argument, what makes it impossible for an eternal power to produce a temporal effect?
A) The power would become non-eternal after creation.
B) The effect would then be eternal, which is impossible.
C) After creation, the eternal relation between power and effect would cease, but an eternal cannot cease.
D) The power would be insufficient to create at a later time.

Key Takeaway
Eternal power cannot be directed at a temporal effect because the relation would have to cease, contradicting the eternality of that relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's eternal power cannot directly cause a temporal effect without contradiction, how does that affect the way you think about your prayers being answered at specific times? Are you willing to trust that His power works through a different modality than you instinctively assume?
?
Reflect on a time when you tried to 'force' something to happen that God had already decreed — like demanding a sign when He had already given you guidance. How does the impossibility of bringing into existence what already exists expose the futility of such impatience?
?
The argument shows that an eternal connection cannot cease. What does this imply about your own attachment to worldly things that are temporary? Do you ever treat temporal blessings as if they are eternal, forgetting that they can and will cease?

The True Meaning of Causing Existence (al-Mūjidiyya)
Razi argues why al-mūjidiyya is not the same as the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'causing existence' is a property of the agent, not the effect, and why the agent's act is always by way of giving existence.

Core Idea
Causing existence is a property of the agent, not identical to the effect.

This passage from Imam Razi's Muhassal tackles a subtle but crucial point about divine causation. Before we can discuss how God creates, we need to be clear on what it means to 'cause existence.' Razi shows that confusing the cause with the effect leads to logical absurdity. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the structure: Razi presents two arguments and a conclusion.
The passage uses logical deduction.
2
Understand the first argument
Razi's first point: existence is a property of what exists, and the effect may not be a property of the agent. For example, the world is not a property of God. So 'causing existence' cannot be the same as the effect.
3
Understand the second argument and conclusion
The second argument shows that if 'causing existence' were identical to the effect, then saying 'the agent caused it' would be tautological, implying the effect caused itself. Therefore, causing existence is a property of the agent, and the agent must act by giving existence.
This establishes the term 'īṣād' (إيصاد).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إذا قلنا القادر يمكنه أن يوجد فالموجدية ليست عبارة عن نفس الأثر ، أما أولا فلان الموجودية صفة الموجود والأثر قد لا يكون صفة له ، فإن العالم ليست صفة الله تعالى ، وأما ثانيا فلانا إذا قلنا الأثر إنما وجد بالقادر لأن القادر أوجده فلو كان المفهوم من قولنا أوجده نفس وجود الأثر ، لكنا قد قلنا إنما وجد الأثر الأنه وجد الأثر فيكون الحاصل أنه وجد الأثر بنفسه ، وذلك محال ، فظهر أن الموجودية صفة للموجد فهي إن كانت مسكنة الوجود واقعة بالقادر المختار عاد التقسيم فيه وإن كانت واجبة وجب وجود الأثر لأن الموجدية بدون وجود الأثر البتة محال عقلا ، فثبت أن المؤثر لا يفعل إلا على سبيل الإيصاد
Fourth: if we say that the powerful can cause to exist, then the property of being the cause of existence is not the same as the effect itself. First, because existence is a property of what exists, and the effect may not be a property of it—for example, the world is not a property of God. Second, if we say the effect exists by the powerful because the powerful caused it to exist, if the meaning of 'He caused it to exist' were the same as the existence of the effect, then we would have said 'the effect exists because it exists,' which would mean that it exists by itself, and that is impossible. So it becomes clear that 'causing existence' is a property of the one who causes existence. So if it is a possible property and occurs by the powerful agent, then the division returns there; and if it is necessary, then the existence of the effect would be necessary because the existence of the cause of existence without the effect is absolutely impossible. So it is established that the effective agent only acts by way of causing existence.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage defines al-mūjidiyya (the property of causing existence) as a property of the agent, not the effect. The author first shows that the effect's existence is a property of the effect, and the effect (like the world) is not a property of God. Second, if causing existence were identical to the effect's existence, the statement 'the agent caused the effect' would be tautological, implying the effect caused itself—an impossibility. Therefore, causing existence is a relational property of the agent. If it is contingent, it falls under the same analysis as other contingent things; if it is necessary, then the effect must exist necessarily. But the effect's existence is not necessary, so causing existence is a contingent relation. This establishes that the agent's act is always by way of giving existence (إيصاد).

Key Terms
موجدية
أثر
صفة
إضافة
إيصاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'causing existence' is the same as the effect that comes into being.
Correction: The passage shows that confusing them leads to tautology or self-causation, and that causing existence is a property of the agent, not the effect.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that al-mūjidiyya is not the same as the effect?
A) Because the effect is temporal.
B) Because if it were, the statement 'the agent caused the effect' would be tautological.
C) Because God is not an effect.
D) Because existence is a property of God.

Key Takeaway
Causing existence (al-mūjidiyya) is a property of the agent that is distinct from the effect, and the agent's act is always by way of giving existence (īṣād).

The First Objection on Choice and Its Resolution
Commentator summarizes objection and provides detailed response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against divine choice and the commentator's multi-part response, including the impossibility of a necessitating cause, the refutation of an intermediary, and the proper definition of choice.

Core Idea
Necessitating causality leads to impossible consequences, proving the Creator must be voluntary.

This passage presents a deep theological debate on whether the world's temporality indicates a voluntary creator. The commentator summarizes an objection based on the impossibility of an eternal act, then provides a multi-layered response, arguing that a necessitating cause would entail beginningless events, which is impossible. He also refutes the idea of an intermediary between God and the world, and clarifies that choice involves motive-driven necessity without negating free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally before analyzing.
The style is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the objection and its core reasoning
Find the summary of the first objection: that temporality does not indicate choice because a necessitating cause would face the same issue. Understand why the objector thinks this.
3
Follow the commentator's answer
Trace the answer: if the cause were necessitating, the effect must accompany it eternally; any delay requires conditions leading to an infinite regress. This is impossible.
This refutes the objection.
4
Understand the refutation of the intermediary
Note the critique of using consensus to refute the intermediary. The commentator provides a rational argument: the intermediary cannot be necessary (only one Necessary Being exists) and must be part of the world, so it cannot mediate between God and the world.
5
Grasp the correct definition of choice
Examine the final section: choice means the agent's power is equally related to both alternatives, and the motive (dā'ī) makes one side preponderant. This necessity from motive does not negate free will.
This resolves the initial tension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : تلخيص الاعتراض الأول هو أن الحديث لا يدل على الاختيار ، فإن الأثر مع وجود القدرة والداعي لو كان ممتنعا لإمتناع دهوة الداعى إلى الوجود لكان مع المؤثر الموجب أيضاً ممتنعا لإمتناع تحصيل الحاصل ، فإذا الحدوث غير دال على الاختيار ، بل كما وجب أن يقع مع المختار وجب أن يقع مع الموج فإن إمتناع كون الفعل أزليا دائر معهما على السواء . يتبع وجوابه : أن مقارنة الأم المؤثر الموجب واجب وليس بتحصيل الحاصل بل هو حصول يجب ان حصو لا آخر ، وتخلفه لا يمكن إلا بسبب وقوعه على شرط غير المفارن فعدم مقارنته له يكون بسبب شرط آخر ويلزم حوادث لا أول لها . والحاصل أن المؤثر إن كان موجبا كان العالم إما قديما وإما محدثا موقوف على حوادث لا أول لها بينا إمتناع كونه قديما وإمتناع وجود حوادث لا أول لها إمتنع كونه موجبا وحينئذ وجب كونه مختاراً للقسمة الخاصرة لهما. وأما إبطال الواسطة بإجماع المسلمين فليس كما ينبغي والمعتمد في إبطالها أن الواسطة يمتنع أن تكون واجبة الوجود لإمتناع أن يكون الواجب أكثر من واحد فإذا هي ممكنة وهى من جملة العالم ، لأن المراد من العالم ما سوى المبدأ الأول فإذن وقوع الواسطة بين واجب الوجود لذاته وبين العالم محال . والمعارضة الأولى من النوع الأول مدفوعة عند المحققين من المتكلمين لا بما دفعه هو من القول بترجح أحد مقدورى المختار من غير مرجح، بل بأن معنى استجماع المصدر جميع ما لابد منه في المصدرية هو بأن يكون المؤثر المختار مأخوذاً مع قدرته التى يستوى بالقياس إليها الطرفان ومع داعيه الذى يترجح أحد الطرفين ، وحينئذ يجب وقوع الفعل بعدهما ، ولا ينافي وجودية الاختيار ، فإن معنى الاختيار هو استواء الطرفين بالقياس إلى القدرة وحدها ، ووقوع العارف الذى يتعلق به الداعى، وهذا كما إذا فرضنا وقوع الفعل من المختار كان وجوب الفعل من جهة فرض الوقوع لا ينافى الاختيار وبذلك بطل قوله . فثبت أن المسكنة من الفعل والترك غير معتبرة في حقيقة القادر ولم يلزم من ذلك وقوع الفعل بمجرد الانفاق ، واتضح الوجه في الجواب عن الأمثلة التي أوردها في المذاهب، فإن المكنة في جميعها حاصلة باعتبار القدرة ، والوجوب واقع باعتبار الإرادة والعلم
I say: summary of the first objection is that the tradition does not indicate choice; for if the effect, given the presence of power and motive, were impossible because it would require the motive to be to nonexistence, then with the necessitating cause it would also be impossible because it would require the acquisition of what is already acquired. So temporality does not indicate choice; rather, just as it must occur with the voluntary agent, it must occur with the necessitating agent, because the impossibility of the act being eternal applies equally to both. Its answer: that the accompaniment of the effect with the necessitating cause is necessary and is not an acquisition of what is acquired; rather it is an occurrence that must occur in another moment; its delay cannot be except due to its occurrence on a condition that is not permanent, so its non-accompaniment with it is due to another condition, and this leads to beginningless temporal events. And the conclusion: if the cause were necessitating, then the world would be either eternal or temporally originated dependent on beginningless events; we have shown the impossibility of its being eternal and the impossibility of beginningless events, so its being necessitating is impossible; then it must be voluntary, due to the division that excludes both. As for refuting the intermediary by consensus of Muslims, that is not as it should be; the reliable refutation is that the intermediary cannot be necessary in existence because it is impossible for there to be more than one necessary being; so it is possible and part of the world, because the world means everything besides the First Principle. So the occurrence of an intermediary between the Necessary Existence and the world is impossible. The first objection from the first type is refuted by the investigators among the theologians not by his own refutation of saying that one of the two possibilities of the voluntary agent is preponderant without a preponderant, but rather by the meaning of the source possessing all that is necessary for sourcehood: it is that the voluntary agent is taken together with his power, in relation to which both sides are equal, and with his motive which makes one side preponderant; then the act must occur after both, and this does not contradict the reality of choice, for the meaning of choice is the equality of the two sides with respect to power alone, and the occurrence of the object that the motive attaches to. This is like when we assume the occurrence of the act from the voluntary agent; the necessity of the act from the perspective of assuming occurrence does not contradict choice. With that his statement is refuted. So it is established that the possibility of act and omission is not considered in the reality of the powerful; and it does not follow from that that the act occurs merely by agreement. And the way of answering the examples he mentioned in the schools is clear, for the possibility in all of them is obtained by virtue of power, and the necessity occurs by virtue of will and knowledge.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This is a detailed commentary on the first objection regarding divine choice. The commentator summarizes the objection: the temporality of the world does not indicate voluntary choice because a necessitating cause would also face the same impossibility of an eternal act. The answer shows that a necessitating cause would require the effect to accompany it eternally; if not, additional conditions would lead to an infinite regress of events. Therefore, the cause must be voluntary. The commentator also critiques the previous refutation of the intermediary (al-wāsiṭa), offering a rational argument instead of consensus: the intermediary cannot be necessary (since only one necessary being exists), so it is part of the world, making it impossible to be between God and the world. Finally, he clarifies the correct understanding of choice: the agent's power is neutral, and the motive determines the act; this does not negate choice because the necessity arises from the motive, not from the power alone. Thus, the possibility of act or omission is not essential to being powerful.

Key Terms
اختيار
موجب
داعٍ
قدرة
ترجيح
مرجح
إرادة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the act is necessary given a motive, it negates free will.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the necessity arises from the motive, not from power alone; choice consists of the equality of power with respect to both alternatives, so motive-based necessity does not negate free will.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the meaning of choice (ikhtiyār)?
A) The ability to do both actions equally.
B) The agent's power being equal for both sides with motive determining one side.
C) Doing an action without any cause.
D) The act being necessary in all respects.

Key Takeaway
The commentator establishes that true choice involves power being equally related to both alternatives, while the motive gives necessity to one side, preserving both divine freedom and rational grounding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the cause of the world must be voluntary because a necessitating cause leads to absurdities. But do you live as though your own choices are merely products of external causes? When will you take full responsibility for a decision, acknowledging that your power is neutral and your motive is your own?
?
The commentator argues that possibility of act and omission is not essential to being powerful. Does your life reflect that you believe you are free only when you could have done otherwise? What if true power is the ability to act decisively once will and knowledge incline you—are you willing to commit to that kind of freedom?
?
The objection claims that temporality does not indicate choice, because a necessitating cause could also produce a temporal effect. How does this challenge your confidence in arguments for God's will? Will you now examine your own reasoning about causality and choice more carefully, not settling for superficial answers?

Answers to Objections on Divine Power
The author answers four objections about God's power and relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari responses to four objections concerning divine power, focusing on definitions of qudra, nisba, ta'allaq, and moojidiyya.

Core Idea
Divine power relates to future possibilities; relations like correlation have no concrete existence.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi answers four objections that challenge the Ash'ari view of God's power. He provides concise definitions and refutes claims of ontologically real relations. This lesson unpacks each answer.

Guided Path
1
Answer the third objection: Define qadir
Read the first answer: God is powerful (qadir) because He can validly produce what is possible in itself. Acts are only valid in the future.
Notice that power is defined by possibility, not by a present connection.
2
Answer the fourth objection: Refute the nisba
Read the second answer: The claimed relation (nisba) that distinguishes the powerful is refuted. Only power (qudra) and the empowered (maqdur) exist.
This rejects any additional ontological entity besides power and its object.
3
Answer the fifth objection: Ta'allaq as relation
Read the third answer: Correlation (ta'allaq) is a relation (idafa) with no existence in concrete entities. Denying it does not entail the non-existence of the eternal God.
Relations are mentally posited, not real.
4
Answer the sixth objection: Moojidiyya as relation
Read the fourth answer: Causing-to-exist (moojidiyya) is a relation of the essence to the effect. Like other relations, it has no concrete existence.
Thus, all four objections are answered by denying extra-mental reality to relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا لا نسلم . ولم لا يجوز أن يقال حصل في الحال التمكن من إيجاده في المستقبل. وأما الثالثة : فجوابها أن القادر هو الذي يصح أن يصدر عنه ما يكون فى نفسه ممكنا ، والفعل إنما يصح فيما لا يزال ، فلا جرم كان الله قادراً فى الأزل على التكوين فيما لا يزال . وأما الرابعة : فجوابها أن النسبة التي أدعيتموها وبنيتم عليها الإمتياز منوعة، فليس في الوجود إلا القدرة والمقدور. وأما الخامسة : فجوابها أن التعاق إضافة ولا وجودها في الأعيان فلا يلزم عدم القديم . وأما السادسة : فجوابها أن الموجدية إضافة الذات إلى الأثر والإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان
We say: we do not accept. And why is it not permissible to say that in the present state there is ability to bring it into existence in the future? As for the third: its answer is that the powerful (al-qadir) is the one from whom it is valid to issue what is possible in itself, and the act is only valid in what is not yet (al-mustaqbal), so necessarily God was powerful in pre-eternity over bringing into existence in the future. As for the fourth: its answer is that the relation (al-nisba) which you claimed and upon which you built the distinction is refuted; there is nothing in existence except power (al-qudra) and the empowered (al-maqdur). As for the fifth: its answer is that correlation (al-ta'allaq) is a relation, and it has no existence in concrete entities (al-a'yan), so the non-existence of the eternal is not necessary. As for the sixth: its answer is that causing-to-exist (al-moojidiyya) is a relation of the essence to the effect, and relations have no existence in concrete entities.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents answers to four objections (the third to sixth) concerning divine power (qudra). The first answer (third objection) defines al-qadir: one who can validly produce what is possible in itself. Acts are only possible in the future, so God, being powerful in pre-eternity, can create in the future. The second answer (fourth objection) refutes the claim of a special relation (nisba) that distinguishes the powerful; only power and the empowered exist. The third answer (fifth objection) deals with correlation (ta'allaq) as a relation with no concrete existence, so denying it does not entail the non-existence of the eternal God. The fourth answer (sixth objection) treats causing-to-exist (moojidiyya) as a relation of essence to effect, which also has no concrete existence. Thus, all objections are repelled by emphasizing that relations are ontologically dependent and not independently existing.

قدرة
مقدور
نسبة
تعاق
موجدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine power requires a present-time connection to the object of power.
Correction: God's power is defined by the possibility of the act in the future; it does not depend on a contemporaneous relation.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the fourth objection regarding the relation (nisba) that supposedly distinguishes the powerful?
A) The relation is necessary for power.
B) The relation is refuted; only power and the empowered exist.
C) The relation is eternal.
D) The relation is a divine attribute.

Key Takeaway
The four objections are answered by clarifying that divine power is defined by future possibility and that relations like nisba, ta'allaq, and moojidiyya have no concrete existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power is not tied to a present-time connection, then your prayers for the future are already within His power from pre-eternity. Does this truth change how you approach your requests to God today, or do you still act as if He needs time to prepare?
?
The source teaches that causing-to-exist is merely a relation with no concrete existence. Have you ever measured your faith by how 'connected' you feel to God — by a feeling or a relational sensation — rather than by trusting in His actual power, which is independent of any emotional state? Will you repent of this and rely on His power alone?
?
The objection about correlation (ta'allaq) arises from a desire to grasp how God relates to creation. But the answer says relations have no concrete existence. Are you willing to accept that some aspects of God's action are beyond your comprehension, or do you secretly demand a tangible explanation before you trust? What step will you take this week to trust God's power without needing to understand the 'how'?

The Necessitarian Absurdity
The author summarizes the objection and refutes it via a dichotomy leading to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessitarian view of divine causality leads to logical absurdities, forcing the conclusion that God must be a free agent.

Core Idea
If God were a necessitarian, the world must be eternal or infinite—both impossible.

The objector claims that the world's temporality does not prove free will; both a free agent and a necessitarian could produce a temporal world. The author turns the tables by examining what necessitarianism really entails. He shows that it inevitably collapses into either an eternal world or an infinite regress of events—both rationally impossible. Thus, only free will remains.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection summary
Read the first part of the passage where the author summarizes the objection: the effect is equally necessary with a free agent or a necessitarian.
Notice the phrase 'tahsil al-hasil' (attaining the attained) which is a key concept.
2
Understand the answer's structure
The author presents a conditional argument: if the cause is necessitarian, the world is either eternal or dependent on infinite events. Both are impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since both options are impossible, the cause cannot be a necessitarian; it must be a free agent.
The author calls this 'the exclusive division of the two'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : تلخيص الاعتراض الأول هو أن الحديث لا يدل على الاختيار ، فإن الأثر مع وجود القدرة والداعي لو كان ممتنعا لإمتناع دهوة الداعى إلى الوجود لكان مع المؤثر الموجب أيضاً ممتنعا لإمتناع تحصيل الحاصل ، فإذا الحدوث غير دال على الاختيار ، بل كما وجب أن يقع مع المختار وجب أن يقع مع الموج فإن إمتناع كون الفعل أزليا دائر معهما على السواء . يتبع وجوابه : أن مقارنة الأم المؤثر الموجب واجب وليس بتحصيل الحاصل بل هو حصول يجب ان حصو لا آخر ، وتخلفه لا يمكن إلا بسبب وقوعه على شرط غير المفارن فعدم مقارنته له يكون بسبب شرط آخر ويلزم حوادث لا أول لها . والحاصل أن المؤثر إن كان موجبا كان العالم إما قديما وإما محدثا موقوف على حوادث لا أول لها بينا إمتناع كونه قديما وإمتناع وجود حوادث لا أول لها إمتنع كونه موجبا وحينئذ وجب كونه مختاراً للقسمة الخاصرة لهما. وأما إبطال الواسطة بإجماع المسلمين فليس كما ينبغي والمعتمد في إبطالها أن الواسطة يمتنع أن تكون واجبة الوجود لإمتناع أن يكون الواجب أكثر من واحد فإذا هي ممكنة وهى من جملة العالم ، لأن المراد من العالم ما سوى المبدأ الأول فإذن وقوع الواسطة بين واجب الوجود لذاته وبين العالم محال . والمعارضة الأولى من النوع الأول مدفوعة عند المحققين من المتكلمين لا بما دفعه هو من القول بترجح أحد مقدورى المختار من غير مرجح، بل بأن معنى استجماع المصدر جميع ما لابد منه في المصدرية هو بأن يكون المؤثر المختار مأخوذاً مع قدرته التى يستوى بالقياس إليها الطرفان ومع داعيه الذى يترجح أحد الطرفين ، وحينئذ يجب وقوع الفعل بعدهما ، ولا ينافي وجودية الاختيار ، فإن معنى الاختيار هو استواء الطرفين بالقياس إلى القدرة وحدها ، ووقوع العارف الذى يتعلق به الداعى، وهذا كما إذا فرضنا وقوع الفعل من المختار كان وجوب الفعل من جهة فرض الوقوع لا ينافى الاختيار وبذلك بطل قوله . فثبت أن المسكنة من الفعل والترك غير معتبرة في حقيقة القادر ولم يلزم من ذلك وقوع الفعل بمجرد الانفاق ، واتضح الوجه في الجواب عن الأمثلة التي أوردها في المذاهب، فإن المكنة في جميعها حاصلة باعتبار القدرة ، والوجوب واقع باعتبار الإرادة والعلم
I say: A summary of the first objection is that the [divine] speech does not indicate choice, for the effect, given the existence of power and motive, if it were impossible due to the impossibility of the motive's call to existence, then with the necessitating efficient cause it would also be impossible due to the impossibility of attaining the attained (tahsil al-hasil). So temporality does not indicate choice; rather, just as it necessarily occurs with the free agent, it necessarily occurs with the necessitarian, because the impossibility of the act being eternal applies equally to both. To be continued. And its answer: that the concomitance of the matter with the necessitating efficient cause is necessary, and it is not attaining the attained, but rather it is a necessary attainment of a latter attainment, and its delay is only possible due to its occurrence on a condition not always concomitant; so its non-concomitance is due to another condition, and this entails incalculable events. The upshot: if the efficient cause were a necessitarian, the world would be either eternal or temporal dependent on incalculable events; since eternalness is impossible and the existence of incalculable events is impossible, its being a necessitarian is impossible, and then it must be a free agent by the exclusive division of the two. As for the refutation of the intermediary by the consensus of Muslims, it is not as it should be, and the basis for refuting it is that the intermediary cannot be necessary in existence because the necessary cannot be more than one, so it is possible and part of the world, for the world means whatever is other than the First Principle; therefore, the occurrence of an intermediary between the Necessary Existence in itself and the world is impossible. The first objection of the first type is refuted by the verified scholars of kalam, not by what he refuted—the claim of the preponderance of one of the two possible objects of the free agent without a preponderator—but rather by the meaning of the source gathering all that is necessary for sourcehood: that the free efficient cause is taken with His power, in relation to which both sides are equal, and with His motive, by which one side is preponderated; then the occurrence of the act after both is necessary, and this does not contradict the reality of choice, for the meaning of choice is the equality of the two sides in relation to power alone, and the occurrence of the act to which the motive is attached. This is like when we suppose the occurrence of the act from the free agent, the necessity of the act from the perspective of the supposition of occurrence does not contradict choice; thereby his statement is invalidated. So it is established that possibility (al-makāna) of act and omission is not considered in the reality of the powerful, and it does not follow from that the occurrence of the act merely by absoluteness; and the way of answering the examples he brought in the schools is clear, for the possibility (al-makāna) in all of them is obtained by virtue of power, and the necessity is actual by virtue of will and knowledge.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a summary and refutation of an objection against divine free choice (ikhtiyār). The objection claims that the temporality of the world does not prove choice because both a free agent and a necessitarian cause would produce a temporal effect equally. The author answers by analyzing the necessitarian model: if God were a necessitarian (mūjib), then either the world would be eternal (impossible) or it would be temporal but dependent on an infinite regress of events (impossible). Thus, the necessitarian view is false, and God must be a free agent. The author also refutes the possibility of an intermediary between God and the world, arguing that only God is necessary; any intermediary is possible and thus part of the world. Finally, he clarifies that the verified scholars refute the objection not by appealing to a preponderator without ground, but by distinguishing power and motive: power alone is neutral, but motive determines the act, and the act follows necessarily after both. This necessity does not negate choice because choice is defined as the equality of power, not the absence of necessity given motive. The examples from other schools are answered similarly: possibility is from power, necessity from will and knowledge.

Key Terms
موجب
تحصيل الحاصل
حوادث لا أول لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the temporality of the world could equally be explained by a necessitarian cause, so free will is not proven.
Correction: The author shows that necessitarianism leads to impossible outcomes (eternal world or infinite regress), so only free will remains valid.

Checkpoint
What two impossible outcomes does the author identify if the cause were a necessitarian?
A) The world would be eternal or require an infinite regress of events.
B) The world would be temporal or require a single intermediary.
C) The world would be created from nothing or from pre-existent matter.
D) The world would be perfect or imperfect.

Key Takeaway
Necessitarianism is impossible because it forces the world to be either eternal or dependent on an infinite series, so the cause must be a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God's actions are necessary and predictable, or do you truly believe He is a free agent who can act as He wills? What area of your life reveals your actual belief?
?
If you genuinely believed that every moment of your existence is sustained by a free, personal choice of God rather than a blind necessity, how would your sense of gratitude and humility change today?
?
The argument shows that denying God's free will leads to absurdities. But you might still act as if the universe runs on automatic – your habits, your prayers, your trust. Where have you been living like a necessitarian, and what step will you take today to acknowledge God's free, personal involvement in your life?

Refuting the Intermediary
The author refutes the possibility of an intermediary between God and the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why there cannot be an intermediary (wāsita) between God and the world, and how this refutes certain objections.

Core Idea
No intermediary can exist between the Necessary Being and the world.

Some thinkers posited an intermediary between God and the world to explain creation. The author shows this is impossible: any intermediary would either be necessary (contradicting God's uniqueness) or possible (thus part of the world). The argument is brief but decisive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The author mentions 'the refutation of the intermediary by the consensus of Muslims' but says it's not sufficient; he gives a rational argument.
The intermediary concept was used by some philosophers.
2
Understand the two-part argument
The intermediary cannot be necessary in existence because only God is necessary. So it must be possible. But possible beings are part of the world (everything other than God).
Thus the intermediary would be part of the world, not between God and world.
3
Grasp the conclusion
An intermediary between the Necessary Being and the world is impossible.
This supports the direct creation by a free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Choice and Necessity: Reconciling Power and Motive
The author refutes an objection by clarifying the definition of choice and its compatibility with necessity from motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verified scholars of kalam define choice such that the necessity of an act given motive does not negate free will.

Core Idea
Choice means power is neutral; necessity from motive does not contradict it.

A key objection claims that if an act is necessary after motive, then free will is negated. The author explains that the verified scholars answer differently: choice is defined by the equality of power over opposites, not by the absence of necessity once motive is present. The act is necessary after motive, but this necessity is compatible with choice.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection type
The objector says that if the act is necessary given motive, then the agent is not truly free. The author says the verified scholars do not reply by claiming a preponderator without ground.
This refers to the Ash'ari position of 'preponderance without a preponderator'.
2
Grasp the definition of choice
The author defines choice (ikhtiyār) as the equality of two sides with respect to power alone. Then the motive comes and determines one side, making the act necessary.
Power is the ability to do or not do; motive tips the scale.
3
See the compatibility
The necessity of the act after motive does not contradict choice because choice is not defined by the absence of necessity, but by the neutrality of power. The supposition of the act occurring does not negate that the agent could have done otherwise in terms of power.
The author gives an analogy: if we suppose the act occurs, it is necessary from that perspective, but still chosen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : تلخيص الاعتراض الأول هو أن الحديث لا يدل على الاختيار ، فإن الأثر مع وجود القدرة والداعي لو كان ممتنعا لإمتناع دهوة الداعى إلى الوجود لكان مع المؤثر الموجب أيضاً ممتنعا لإمتناع تحصيل الحاصل ، فإذا الحدوث غير دال على الاختيار ، بل كما وجب أن يقع مع المختار وجب أن يقع مع الموج فإن إمتناع كون الفعل أزليا دائر معهما على السواء . يتبع وجوابه : أن مقارنة الأم المؤثر الموجب واجب وليس بتحصيل الحاصل بل هو حصول يجب ان حصو لا آخر ، وتخلفه لا يمكن إلا بسبب وقوعه على شرط غير المفارن فعدم مقارنته له يكون بسبب شرط آخر ويلزم حوادث لا أول لها . والحاصل أن المؤثر إن كان موجبا كان العالم إما قديما وإما محدثا موقوف على حوادث لا أول لها بينا إمتناع كونه قديما وإمتناع وجود حوادث لا أول لها إمتنع كونه موجبا وحينئذ وجب كونه مختاراً للقسمة الخاصرة لهما. وأما إبطال الواسطة بإجماع المسلمين فليس كما ينبغي والمعتمد في إبطالها أن الواسطة يمتنع أن تكون واجبة الوجود لإمتناع أن يكون الواجب أكثر من واحد فإذا هي ممكنة وهى من جملة العالم ، لأن المراد من العالم ما سوى المبدأ الأول فإذن وقوع الواسطة بين واجب الوجود لذاته وبين العالم محال . والمعارضة الأولى من النوع الأول مدفوعة عند المحققين من المتكلمين لا بما دفعه هو من القول بترجح أحد مقدورى المختار من غير مرجح، بل بأن معنى استجماع المصدر جميع ما لابد منه في المصدرية هو بأن يكون المؤثر المختار مأخوذاً مع قدرته التى يستوى بالقياس إليها الطرفان ومع داعيه الذى يترجح أحد الطرفين ، وحينئذ يجب وقوع الفعل بعدهما ، ولا ينافي وجودية الاختيار ، فإن معنى الاختيار هو استواء الطرفين بالقياس إلى القدرة وحدها ، ووقوع العارف الذى يتعلق به الداعى، وهذا كما إذا فرضنا وقوع الفعل من المختار كان وجوب الفعل من جهة فرض الوقوع لا ينافى الاختيار وبذلك بطل قوله . فثبت أن المسكنة من الفعل والترك غير معتبرة في حقيقة القادر ولم يلزم من ذلك وقوع الفعل بمجرد الانفاق ، واتضح الوجه في الجواب عن الأمثلة التي أوردها في المذاهب، فإن المكنة في جميعها حاصلة باعتبار القدرة ، والوجوب واقع باعتبار الإرادة والعلم
I say: A summary of the first objection is that the [divine] speech does not indicate choice, for the effect, given the existence of power and motive, if it were impossible due to the impossibility of the motive's call to existence, then with the necessitating efficient cause it would also be impossible due to the impossibility of attaining the attained (tahsil al-hasil). So temporality does not indicate choice; rather, just as it necessarily occurs with the free agent, it necessarily occurs with the necessitarian, because the impossibility of the act being eternal applies equally to both. To be continued. And its answer: that the concomitance of the matter with the necessitating efficient cause is necessary, and it is not attaining the attained, but rather it is a necessary attainment of a latter attainment, and its delay is only possible due to its occurrence on a condition not always concomitant; so its non-concomitance is due to another condition, and this entails incalculable events. The upshot: if the efficient cause were a necessitarian, the world would be either eternal or temporal dependent on incalculable events; since eternalness is impossible and the existence of incalculable events is impossible, its being a necessitarian is impossible, and then it must be a free agent by the exclusive division of the two. As for the refutation of the intermediary by the consensus of Muslims, it is not as it should be, and the basis for refuting it is that the intermediary cannot be necessary in existence because the necessary cannot be more than one, so it is possible and part of the world, for the world means whatever is other than the First Principle; therefore, the occurrence of an intermediary between the Necessary Existence in itself and the world is impossible. The first objection of the first type is refuted by the verified scholars of kalam, not by what he refuted—the claim of the preponderance of one of the two possible objects of the free agent without a preponderator—but rather by the meaning of the source gathering all that is necessary for sourcehood: that the free efficient cause is taken with His power, in relation to which both sides are equal, and with His motive, by which one side is preponderated; then the occurrence of the act after both is necessary, and this does not contradict the reality of choice, for the meaning of choice is the equality of the two sides in relation to power alone, and the occurrence of the act to which the motive is attached. This is like when we suppose the occurrence of the act from the free agent, the necessity of the act from the perspective of the supposition of occurrence does not contradict choice; thereby his statement is invalidated. So it is established that possibility (al-makāna) of act and omission is not considered in the reality of the powerful, and it does not follow from that the occurrence of the act merely by absoluteness; and the way of answering the examples he brought in the schools is clear, for the possibility (al-makāna) in all of them is obtained by virtue of power, and the necessity is actual by virtue of will and knowledge.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a summary and refutation of an objection against divine free choice (ikhtiyār). The objection claims that the temporality of the world does not prove choice because both a free agent and a necessitarian cause would produce a temporal effect equally. The author answers by analyzing the necessitarian model: if God were a necessitarian (mūjib), then either the world would be eternal (impossible) or it would be temporal but dependent on an infinite regress of events (impossible). Thus, the necessitarian view is false, and God must be a free agent. The author also refutes the possibility of an intermediary between God and the world, arguing that only God is necessary; any intermediary is possible and thus part of the world. Finally, he clarifies that the verified scholars refute the objection not by appealing to a preponderator without ground, but by distinguishing power and motive: power alone is neutral, but motive determines the act, and the act follows necessarily after both. This necessity does not negate choice because choice is defined as the equality of power, not the absence of necessity given motive. The examples from other schools are answered similarly: possibility is from power, necessity from will and knowledge.

Key Terms
اختيار
داع
مكنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if an act is necessary after motive, then free will is negated.
Correction: Choice is defined by the equality of power over opposites, not by the absence of necessity. Necessity from motive is compatible with choice.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of choice (ikhtiyār) according to the verified scholars?
A) The ability to do or not do without any cause.
B) The equality of the two sides in relation to power alone.
C) The absence of any necessity.
D) The act occurring without a motive.

Key Takeaway
Choice is defined by power's neutrality; necessity from motive does not negate free will, so the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you tend to excuse yourself by saying 'I couldn't help it'? How does this lesson's definition of choice—as power's neutrality even when motive is present—challenge that excuse and call you to own your motives?
?
Consider a time you obeyed Allah despite strong contrary desires. According to this lesson, your power was neutral, and your motive (dāʿī) for obedience preponderated. Does knowing that your act was necessary given your motive make you feel less responsible? Or does it press you to examine what motives you cultivate?
?
If you truly believe that your choices are not negated by the necessity that follows your motives, what does that demand of you in terms of guarding your heart’s desires? Are you actively shaping your motives to align with what pleases Allah, or are you passively following whatever desire is strongest?

The Proof from Precise Creation
Al-Razi states the proof and refines the meaning of 'precise act'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main syllogistic proof for God's omniscience and the initial objections about the meaning of 'precise act'.

Core Idea
The world's precision points to a knowing Creator, but defining 'precision' is key.

Imam al-Razi presents the consensus that God is all-knowing, except for ancient philosophers. He argues from the perfection of creation. However, skeptics question whether such precision truly indicates knowledge. This lesson unpacks the main syllogism and the first set of objections about benefit and convention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style and the dialectical structure.
2
Identify the two premises of the proof
The proof has a sensory premise and a self-evident premise. Find them in the text and note how they are described.
The first premise is that God's actions are precise; the second is that everything with precise actions is all-knowing.
3
Analyze the three meanings of 'precise act'
The author considers three possible interpretations: beneficial, conventionally good, or a third unknown. For each, he examines sub-meanings and shows why they fail or are insufficient.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'in every respect' and 'in some respects'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اتفق جمهور العقلاء على أنه تعالى عالم إلا قدماء الفلاسفة لها أفعاله محكمة متقنة فكل ما كان كذلك فهو عالم ، والمقدمة الأولى حسية ، والثانية بديهية ، فإن قبل لا نعلم أن هذا العالم فعله ، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون فعل الواسطة ، سلمناه لكن المراد من الفعل المحكم هو الذي يكون مطابقا للمنفعة أو ما يكون مستحسنا في العرف أو أمر ثالثا ، فإن أردتم به الأول فإما أن تريدوا به كون الفعل مطابقا للمنفعة من كل الوجوه أو من بعض الوجوه ، فإن أردت به الأول فهو ممنوع ولم قلتم إن المخلوقات مطابقة المنفعة من كل الوجوه فظاهر أنها ليست كذلك لكثرة ما نشاهد في العالم من الآفات ، إن أردت به الثاني فمسلم لكن كون الفعل مشتملا على النفع من بعض الوجوه لا يدل على كون فاعله عالما ، لأن فعل الساهي والنائم بل الحركات الصادرة عن الجمادات قد يكون نافعة من بعض الوجوه ، وأما إن أردتم بالمحكم ما يكون مستحسنا في العرف فإما أن تريد را ، ما يكون مستحسنا على وجه لا يمكن تحور دا هو أحسن منه أو تريدوا به كونه مستحسنا في الجملة ، فإن أردت به الأول فلا نعلم أن العالم كذلك فإنا لا ندرى أن ترتيب الكواكب في السموات أو ترتيب أبدان الحيوانات على وجه أكمل فيهما و الآن عليه ممكن أم لا ، فإن أردت به الثاني فمسلم أن العالم كذلك لكنه لا يدل على الفاعل، فإن فعل الساهي والنائم قد يستحسن من بعض الوجوه ، فأما إن أردت بالإحكام والإتقان معنى ثالثا فاذكروه لنتكلم عليه ، ولئن نزلنا عن الاستفسار فلم قلت إن فعل المحكم يدل على علم الفاعل وبيانه من وجوه . والمعارضة الثانية بأن المنكنة لا تثبت في حال الحصول لأن الحاصل حينئذ واجب ومقابله ممتنع في الحال مدفوعة لما ذكره ، وهو أن الحاصل في الحال هو التمكن من التحصيل فى الاستقبال ، إلا أن ذلك لا يتمشى في قدرة العبد مع القول بكونها مقارنة للفعل . والتحقيق فيه أن الوقوع في الاستقبال ممكن الإجتماع مع وجود المكنة في الحال وممتنع الاجتماع مع الوقوع في الحال حتى لزم منه المحال . والمعارضة الثالثة : بأن القادر على قولكم متردد بين الفعل والترك ، والترك لا يكون مقدوراً فجوابها أن منه أن يفعل وأن لا يفعل لا أن يفعل الترك ، والمصنف أورد في جوابه ما أورده في القادر هو الذى يصح جواب المعارضة الثانية ولكن بعبارة أخرى . وأما ما أورده في النوع الثاني من المعارضة وهو أن التمكن من الأثر يستدعى صحة الأثر ، فالجواب عنه أن المتمكن من التأثير فى الأزل متناقض ولذلك كان المتمكن من التأثير مطلقا مستدعيا لصحة الأثر بعد ذلك . والمعارضة التي بعدها وهى التى سماها عند الجواب بالرابعة ، وهى أن المقدور لابد وأن يكون متميزا من غيره حتى يختص القادر بإيجاده فجوابها أن التميز العقلى كاف ، وجوابه بنفى الأمور النسبية غير نافع ههنا . والمعارضة الموسومة بالخامسة وهى أن تعاق القادر بالمقدور المطلق لايين ، وأما بالمقدور المعين فأمر اضافي وهو الذى سمى بالخالقية وحكمه حكم سائر الاضافات . والمعارضة الأخيرة بأن الموجدية صفة الموجد فهى إن كانت ممكنة الوجود وقمعت بالقادر عاد النقسم ، وار كانت واجبة وجوب وجود الأمر معه فجوابه ماقيل فى الصفات الإضافية
Issue: The majority of rational people agree that He, exalted, is all-knowing, except the ancient philosophers. [Because] His actions are precise and perfect, and everything that is so is all-knowing. The first premise is sensory, the second is self-evident. If it is said: we do not know that this world is His act; why may it not be the act of an intermediary? We accept that, but the meaning of the precise act (al-fi'l al-muhkam) is that which conforms to benefit (al-manfa'a) or what is considered good in custom, or a third meaning. If you intend the first, then either you mean the act conforming to benefit in every respect or in some respects. If you intend the first, it is rejected: why do you claim that creatures conform to benefit in every respect? It is apparent that they are not so, due to the many afflictions we witness in the world. If you intend the second, it is granted, but an act containing benefit in some respects does not indicate that its agent is all-knowing, because the act of the inattentive or sleeping person, even movements from inanimate objects, may be beneficial in some respects. As for if you mean by 'precise' what is considered good in custom, then either you mean what is good in a way that no better is possible, or you mean it is good in a general sense. If you intend the first, we do not know that the world is so, for we do not know whether the arrangement of planets in the heavens or the arrangement of animal bodies is in a way that is more complete than what is possible or not. If you intend the second, it is granted that the world is so, but it does not indicate the agent, for the act of the inattentive or sleeping person may be deemed good in some respects. But if you intend by 'precision and perfection' (al-ihkām wa al-itqān) a third meaning, mention it so we can discuss it. Even if we descend from this inquiry, why do you say that the precise act indicates the knowledge of its agent, and explain it in several ways? The second objection: that possibility (al-makāna) is not established at the moment of acquisition, because what is acquired is then necessary and its opposite is impossible at the moment, is refuted by what he mentioned: that what is acquired in the present is the ability to acquire in the future, except that this does not apply to the power of the servant given the claim that it is concomitant with the act. The investigation is that occurrence in the future is compatible with the existence of possibility in the present but incompatible with occurrence in the present, such that impossible follows. The third objection: that the powerful (al-qādir) according to your statement is hesitant between act and omission, but omission is not an object of power. Its answer is that [the meaning is] that from him it is to act and not to act, not that he makes omission an object of power. The author gave in his answer what he gave in the answer to the second objection, but in another wording. As for what he brought in the second type of objection—that the ability to have an effect demands the validity of the effect—the answer is that the one able to have an effect in pre-eternity is self-contradictory; therefore, the one able to have an effect absolutely demands the validity of the effect afterwards. The objection after that, which he called in the answer the fourth: that the empowered (al-maqdūr) must be distinct from other things so that the powerful is specifically connected to its bringing into existence. Its answer is that intellectual distinction is sufficient, and his answer by denying relational matters is not useful here. The objection labelled the fifth: that the correlation (ta'allaq) of the powerful with the empowered absolutely is not clear; as for with a specific empowered, it is a relational matter, which is called creatorhood (al-khāliqiyya), and its ruling is the ruling of all other relations. The last objection: that causing-to-exist (al-moojidiyya) is an attribute of the causer; if it is possible in existence and associated with the powerful, the division recurs; if it is necessary, then the existence of the thing with it is necessary. Its answer is what was said about relational attributes.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of God's omniscience and responds to a series of objections. The proof is a syllogism: God's acts are precise and perfect (premise 1, sensory), and anything with precise acts is all-knowing (premise 2, self-evident), therefore God is all-knowing. Then objections arise: First, the world might be the act of an intermediary. The author grants this but then analyzes what 'precise act' means—either beneficial in every respect (rejected, as the world has afflictions), beneficial in some respects (granted but insufficient, as inattentive beings can produce occasional benefit), or conventionally good (either absolutely best—unknown if world is so—or generally good—granted but insufficient). Thus the definition of 'precise act' must be clarified. Second objection: possibility (makāna) is not present at the moment of acquisition because the acquired is necessary. The answer: what is acquired is the ability to acquire in the future, which is compatible with present possibility. Third objection: the powerful is said to be hesitant between act and omission, but omission is not an object of power. Answer: the meaning is that he can act or not act, not that he makes omission an object. Fourth objection (of the second type): the ability to have an effect in pre-eternity is contradictory; rather, absolute ability demands the validity of the effect afterwards. Fifth objection: the empowered must be distinct; answer: intellectual distinction suffices. Sixth objection: correlation with an absolute empowered is unclear; with a specific empowered it is a relation called creatorhood. Last objection: causing-to-exist is an attribute; if possible, division recurs; if necessary, the thing is necessary. Answer: it is a relational attribute (see earlier discussion). Thus the proof stands.

Key Terms
عالم
محكم
إتقان
منفعة
استحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The design argument requires the world to be maximally beneficial or beautiful.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even partial benefit or general conventional goodness is insufficient to prove omniscience, because inattentive agents can produce such acts.

Checkpoint
What is the author's immediate response to the objection that the world might be the act of an intermediary?
A) He rejects it outright.
B) He concedes and then analyzes the definition of 'precise'.
C) He argues that intermediaries do not exist.
D) He says it is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The proof that God is all-knowing rests on a self-evident link between precise acts and knowledge, but the definition of 'precise' must be carefully examined to avoid objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a trial that seems pointless or harmful, do you still believe that God's action is precise and purposeful? How can you actively train your heart to see hidden benefit or wisdom in afflictions?
?
The argument from precision invites you to reflect on the intricate design of your own body. Have you ever truly paused to consider that the coordination of your cells and organs points to a knowing Creator? What would change if you lived with that awareness every second?
?
Al-Razi forces us to define 'precise act' carefully. In your daily actions, are you striving for precision and excellence as a reflection of your knowledge of God? Or are you content with sloppiness, forgetting that your own precision points back to the All-Knowing? What is one area of your life where you will commit to greater precision starting today?

Objections on Possibility and Power
Al-Razi refutes objections about possibility and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections concerning possibility (makāna) and power (qudra), and al-Razi's responses based on temporal distinctions.

Core Idea
Possibility and power imply potential, not actual conflict.

After establishing the meaning of 'precise act', al-Razi now faces more technical objections about the nature of possibility and power. Skeptics argue that at the moment of acquisition, possibility vanishes; similarly, they claim that the concept of a powerful agent able to both act and omit is problematic. Al-Razi provides subtle responses based on the distinction between present and future.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and answer
The objection states that possibility (makāna) is not present at the moment of acquisition because what is acquired is necessary. Al-Razi's answer distinguishes present ability from future occurrence.
Note the term 'makāna' (possibility) and how it relates to time.
2
Read the third objection and answer
The third objection says that the powerful (qādir) is hesitant between act and omission, but omission is not an object of power. The answer clarifies that the meaning is 'able to act and able to not act,' not 'able to make omission an object.'
This clarifies that power is about alternatives, not about making omission as a positive act.
3
Examine the second type of objection
This sub-objection argues that ability to have an effect in pre-eternity is contradictory. Al-Razi's answer uses the idea that absolute ability demands the effect later, not immediately.
This ties back to the distinction between pre-eternal ability and temporal effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يجوز أن يكون فعل الواسطة ، سلمناه لكن المراد من الفعل المحكم هو الذي يكون مطابقا للمنفعة أو ما يكون مستحسنا في العرف أو أمر ثالثا ، فإن أردتم به الأول فإما أن تريدوا به كون الفعل مطابقا للمنفعة من كل تريد را ، ما يكون مستحسنا على وجه لا يمكن تحور دا هو أحسن منه أو تريدوا به كونه مستحسنا في الجملة ، فإن أردت به الأول فلا نعلم أن العالم كذلك فإنا لا ندرى أن ترتيب الكواكب في السموات أو ترتيب أبدان الحيوانات على وجه أكمل فيهما و الآن عليه ممكن أم لا ، فإن أردت به الثاني فمسلم أن العالم كذلك لكنه لا يدل على الفاعل، فإن فعل الساهي والنائم قد يستحسن من بعض الوجوه ، فأما إن أردت بالإحكام والإتقان معنى ثالثا فاذكروه لنتكلم عليه ، ولئن نزلنا عن الاستفسار فلم قلت إن فعل المحكم يدل على علم الفاعل وبيانه من وجوه . والمعارضة الثانية بأن المنكنة لا تثبت في حال الحصول لأن الحاصل حينئذ واجب ومقابله ممتنع في الحال مدفوعة لما ذكره ، وهو أن الحاصل في الحال هو التمكن من التحصيل فى الاستقبال ، إلا أن ذلك لا يتمشى في قدرة العبد مع القول بكونها مقارنة للفعل . والتحقيق فيه أن الوقوع في الاستقبال ممكن الإجتماع مع وجود المكنة في الحال وممتنع الاجتماع مع الوقوع في الحال حتى لزم منه المحال . والمعارضة الثالثة : بأن القادر على قولكم متردد بين الفعل والترك ، والترك لا يكون مقدوراً فجوابها أن منه أن يفعل وأن لا يفعل لا أن يفعل الترك ، والمصنف أورد في جوابه ما أورده في القادر هو الذى يصح جواب المعارضة الثانية ولكن بعبارة أخرى . وأما ما أورده في النوع الثاني من المعارضة وهو أن التمكن من الأثر يستدعى صحة الأثر ، فالجواب عنه أن المتمكن من التأثير فى الأزل متناقض ولذلك كان المتمكن من التأثير مطلقا مستدعيا لصحة الأثر بعد ذلك . والمعارضة التي بعدها وهى التى سماها عند الجواب بالرابعة ، وهى أن المقدور لابد وأن يكون متميزا من غيره حتى يختص القادر بإيجاده فجوابها أن التميز العقلى كاف ، وجوابه بنفى الأمور النسبية غير نافع ههنا . والمعارضة الموسومة بالخامسة وهى أن تعاق القادر بالمقدور المطلق لايين ، وأما بالمقدور المعين فأمر اضافي وهو الذى سمى بالخالقية وحكمه حكم سائر الاضافات . والمعارضة الأخيرة بأن الموجدية صفة الموجد فهى إن كانت ممكنة الوجود وقمعت بالقادر عاد النقسم ، وار كانت واجبة وجوب وجود الأمر معه فجوابه ماقيل فى الصفات الإضافية
Issue: The majority of rational people agree that He, exalted, is all-knowing, except the ancient philosophers. [Because] His actions are precise and perfect, and everything that is so is all-knowing. The first premise is sensory, the second is self-evident. If it is said: we do not know that this world is His act; why may it not be the act of an intermediary? We accept that, but the meaning of the precise act (al-fi'l al-muhkam) is that which conforms to benefit (al-manfa'a) or what is considered good in custom, or a third meaning. If you intend the first, then either you mean the act conforming to benefit in every respect or in some respects. If you intend the first, it is rejected: why do you claim that creatures conform to benefit in every respect? It is apparent that they are not so, due to the many afflictions we witness in the world. If you intend the second, it is granted, but an act containing benefit in some respects does not indicate that its agent is all-knowing, because the act of the inattentive or sleeping person, even movements from inanimate objects, may be beneficial in some respects. As for if you mean by 'precise' what is considered good in custom, then either you mean what is good in a way that no better is possible, or you mean it is good in a general sense. If you intend the first, we do not know that the world is so, for we do not know whether the arrangement of planets in the heavens or the arrangement of animal bodies is in a way that is more complete than what is possible or not. If you intend the second, it is granted that the world is so, but it does not indicate the agent, for the act of the inattentive or sleeping person may be deemed good in some respects. But if you intend by 'precision and perfection' (al-ihkām wa al-itqān) a third meaning, mention it so we can discuss it. Even if we descend from this inquiry, why do you say that the precise act indicates the knowledge of its agent, and explain it in several ways? The second objection: that possibility (al-makāna) is not established at the moment of acquisition, because what is acquired is then necessary and its opposite is impossible at the moment, is refuted by what he mentioned: that what is acquired in the present is the ability to acquire in the future, except that this does not apply to the power of the servant given the claim that it is concomitant with the act. The investigation is that occurrence in the future is compatible with the existence of possibility in the present but incompatible with occurrence in the present, such that impossible follows. The third objection: that the powerful (al-qādir) according to your statement is hesitant between act and omission, but omission is not an object of power. Its answer is that [the meaning is] that from him it is to act and not to act, not that he makes omission an object of power. The author gave in his answer what he gave in the answer to the second objection, but in another wording. As for what he brought in the second type of objection—that the ability to have an effect demands the validity of the effect—the answer is that the one able to have an effect in pre-eternity is self-contradictory; therefore, the one able to have an effect absolutely demands the validity of the effect afterwards. The objection after that, which he called in the answer the fourth: that the empowered (al-maqdūr) must be distinct from other things so that the powerful is specifically connected to its bringing into existence. Its answer is that intellectual distinction is sufficient, and his answer by denying relational matters is not useful here. The objection labelled the fifth: that the correlation (ta'allaq) of the powerful with the empowered absolutely is not clear; as for with a specific empowered, it is a relational matter, which is called creatorhood (al-khāliqiyya), and its ruling is the ruling of all other relations. The last objection: that causing-to-exist (al-moojidiyya) is an attribute of the causer; if it is possible in existence and associated with the powerful, the division recurs; if it is necessary, then the existence of the thing with it is necessary. Its answer is what was said about relational attributes.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of God's omniscience and responds to a series of objections. The proof is a syllogism: God's acts are precise and perfect (premise 1, sensory), and anything with precise acts is all-knowing (premise 2, self-evident), therefore God is all-knowing. Then objections arise: First, the world might be the act of an intermediary. The author grants this but then analyzes what 'precise act' means—either beneficial in every respect (rejected, as the world has afflictions), beneficial in some respects (granted but insufficient, as inattentive beings can produce occasional benefit), or conventionally good (either absolutely best—unknown if world is so—or generally good—granted but insufficient). Thus the definition of 'precise act' must be clarified. Second objection: possibility (makāna) is not present at the moment of acquisition because the acquired is necessary. The answer: what is acquired is the ability to acquire in the future, which is compatible with present possibility. Third objection: the powerful is said to be hesitant between act and omission, but omission is not an object of power. Answer: the meaning is that he can act or not act, not that he makes omission an object. Fourth objection (of the second type): the ability to have an effect in pre-eternity is contradictory; rather, absolute ability demands the validity of the effect afterwards. Fifth objection: the empowered must be distinct; answer: intellectual distinction suffices. Sixth objection: correlation with an absolute empowered is unclear; with a specific empowered it is a relation called creatorhood. Last objection: causing-to-exist is an attribute; if possible, division recurs; if necessary, the thing is necessary. Answer: it is a relational attribute (see earlier discussion). Thus the proof stands.

Key Terms

قادر
مقدور
فعل
ترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is possible, it must be actual at some point; similarly, power must be exercised constantly.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that possibility (makāna) refers to future potential, and power (qudra) refers to the capacity to choose between act and omission, not continuous actualization.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is acquired in the present when considering possibility?
A) The actual occurrence of the act.
B) The ability to acquire in the future.
C) The impossibility of the opposite.
D) The necessity of the act.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's responses rely on the distinction between present and future states, showing that possibility and power are not contradictory to the actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's power does not force Him to act? That He can choose to delay an effect? How does that shape your patience when prayers seem unanswered or when evil persists in the world? Will you let this truth free you from demanding immediate results from God?
?
When you consider your own power to act, do you sometimes lament that you cannot do everything at once? How does al-Razi's insight that power includes the ability to omit comfort you in your limited capacity? Are you willing to accept your human limitations as part of God's design, rather than a flaw?
?
The lesson argues that possibility exists now for future occurrences. Are you living today as if you truly hold that tomorrow is possible only by God's power? Or do you assume a certainty that denies your dependence on Him for even the next moment? What concrete step can you take today to acknowledge that your future actions are merely possible, not guaranteed?

Divine Attributes
advanced
5 min
Final Objections: Distinctness, Correlation, and Creating
Al-Razi refutes objections about distinctness, correlation, and the attribute of causing-to-exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final objections regarding distinctness, correlation, and the attribute of causing-to-exist, and how al-Razi resolves them by appealing to intellectual distinction and relational attributes.

Core Idea
The objections are resolved by noting that intellectual distinctness and relational attributes suffice.

In this final part of the discussion, al-Razi addresses three more subtle objections. Skeptics claim that for power to be specific, the empowered must be distinct; that correlation with an absolute empowered is unclear; and that causing-to-exist as an attribute leads to regress. Al-Razi's answers rely on the sufficiency of intellectual distinction and the nature of relational attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth objection and answer
The fourth objection states that the empowered (maqdūr) must be distinct from other things so that the powerful is specifically connected. Al-Razi's answer: intellectual distinction is sufficient.
Note the term 'tamyīz 'aqlī' (intellectual distinction).
2
Read the fifth objection and answer
The fifth objection concerns the correlation (ta'allaq) of the powerful with the empowered. When the empowered is specific, it's a relational matter called creatorhood (khāliqiyya).
Creatorhood is a relational attribute, like other relations.
3
Read the last objection and answer
The last objection: causing-to-exist (moojidiyya) as an attribute leads to a dilemma. The answer: it is a relational attribute, already discussed.
Relational attributes (ṣifāt iḍāfiyya) are key to resolving this.
