Ancient Philosophers and the Consensus on God's Knowledge
Explains ancient philosophers' view on God's knowledge and states the consensus of Muslims and others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view of ancient philosophers on God's knowledge and the universal agreement among Muslims and other religions that God is knowing.

Core Idea
The ancient philosophers denied God's knowledge of others; Muslims and other religions affirm it.

This passage contrasts the position of ancient philosophers with that of Muslims and other religious communities. The commentator first explains the philosophers' reasoning: knowledge implies a relation and multiplicity, which cannot apply to the First Principle. Then he states the consensus that God is knowing, pointing to the evident signs of design in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Focus on understanding the philosophers' argument and the commentator's own conclusion.
Notice the key term 'المبدأ الأول' (the First Principle), which refers to God.
2
Analyze the philosophers' reasoning
Identify the logical steps: knowledge requires a form of the known, which implies a relation. The First Principle has no multiplicity, so He cannot know anything other than Himself. Thus He is not described with knowledge but emanates it.
This is a denial of divine knowledge of particulars.
3
Contrast with the consensus
The commentator then says 'the rest' (Muslims and all religious people) agree that God is knowing. He cites the argument from design (الإحكام والإتقان) as evidence. Reflect on this contrast.
The consensus includes all people of religions, not just Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : قدماء الفلاسفة قالوا العلم حصول صورة المعلوم في العالم ومع ذلك فهو يقتضى إضافة ما للعالم إلى المعلوم ، والعالم والمعلوم إن كانا متغايرين فلابد أن يتصور العالم بصورة المعلوم ولا يمكن أن يعقل المبدأ الأول شيئا من غيره ، وإن كان واحداً فلابد فيه من تغاير اعتبارين حتى يمكن أن يعقل الإضافة بينهما ولا كثرة في المبدأ الأول بوجه من الوجوه فهو لا يوصف بالعلم بوجه بل هو نقيض العلم على الموجودات التي هي معلولاته بل هو يفيض العلم على الموجودات التي هي معلولاته كما يفيض الوجود عليها بهذا مذهبهم . والباقون منا ومن أهل الملل جميعا انفقوا على أنه تعالى عالم ، أما الإحكام والإتقان فقد يظهر لمن يتأمل أحوال الخلق وينظر في تشريح الأعضاء ومنافعها وهيئة الإفلاك ووجود الذيرات العلوية وحركاتها ، وبديها
I say: The ancient philosophers said that knowledge is the acquisition of the form of the known in the knower, and with that it requires some relation of the knower to the known. If the knower and known are distinct, then the knower must conceive the form of the known, and the First Principle cannot conceive anything other than itself. If it is one, then there must be a distinction of two aspects so that the relation between them can be conceived, and there is no multiplicity in the First Principle in any way—so He is not described by knowledge at all; rather, He is the opposite of knowledge with respect to existents that are His effects. Rather, He pours knowledge upon existents that are His effects just as He pours existence upon them—this is their doctrine. The rest, from us and from the people of religions, all agreed that He is knowing. As for precision and mastery, it becomes clear to one who contemplates the conditions of creation and examines the anatomy of organs and their benefits, the configuration of celestial spheres, and the existence of higher particles and their movements, and it is intuitive...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a footnote (أقول) by the commentator explaining the position of the ancient philosophers (قدماء الفلاسفة) on God's knowledge. They held that knowledge involves acquiring the form of the known, which implies a relation; but the First Principle (المبدأ الأول) has no multiplicity, so He cannot know anything other than Himself. He is not described with knowledge at all—rather He emanates knowledge to His effects. The commentator then states that the rest of us (Muslims) and all religious people agree that He is knowing, and points to the argument from design (الإحكام والإتقان) visible in anatomy, celestial spheres, etc., concluding that it is intuitive (بديهي).

?

God's Life: Agreement and Two Interpretations
Razi states the agreement and presents two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical interpretations of God's attribute of life: as a state of the essence or as an extra attribute.

Core Idea
God's 'life' is agreed upon, but its meaning is debated: essence vs. attribute.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi begins a new discussion on the divine attribute of life. He notes that all rational thinkers agree that God is living, but they diverge on what that means. He presents two main interpretations: one reduces life to a mere negation of impossibility, and the other affirms it as a real attribute. Let's explore these views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully to grasp the agreement and the two views.
The OCR has some corruption, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Notice that the first view defines life as the essence being such that knowledge and power are not impossible; the second view posits life as an attribute.
3
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this debate affects the understanding of God's attributes and the Ash'ari-Mu'tazila dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اتفق العقلاء على أنه حي اكهم اختلفوا فى معنى كونه حيا ، فذهب الجمهور من الفلاسفة ومن المعتزلة أبو الحسين البصرى إلى أن معناه هو أنه لا يستحيل أن يكون عالما قادرا فليس هناك إلا الذات المستلزمة لانتفاء الامتناع ، وذهب الجمهور منا ومن المعتزلة إلى أنه صفة
Question: The rational beings agreed that He is living. [But] they differed regarding the meaning of His being living. The majority of philosophers and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila went that its meaning is: it is not impossible that He be knowing and powerful—so there is nothing but the essence necessitating the negation of impossibility. And the majority of us [Ash'aris] and of the Mu'tazila went that it is an attribute.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage introduces a new topic: God's life (حي). Razi states that all rational beings (العقلاء) agree that God is living, but they disagree on its meaning. He presents two views: (1) the majority of philosophers (جمهور الفلاسفة) and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila hold that 'living' means it is not impossible for Him to be knowing and powerful—there is no extra attribute, only the essence which entails the absence of impossibility. (2) The majority of us (Ash'aris) and of the Mu'tazila hold that life is a real attribute (صفة) distinct from the essence. The text cuts off, but it likely goes on to the Ash'ari proof.

Key Terms
حي
لا يستحيل أن يكون عالما قادرا
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life means merely being alive or not dead.
Correction: The debate here is not about whether God is alive, but about the ontological status of life: is it a separate attribute or just a description of the essence's capacity?

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first interpretation of God's being living?
A) He has a life attribute distinct from essence.
B) It means He is not subject to impossibility of knowledge and power.
C) He is the source of all life.
D) He is alive like other beings.

Key Takeaway
The attribute of life is universally affirmed, but its interpretation divides theologians between those who see it as a state of the essence and those who affirm a distinct attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is Living,' do you truly mean that life is a real attribute of His, or do you subconsciously reduce it to a mere possibility? How does your answer affect the way you relate to a Living God?
?
The philosophers argued that life is just the essence not being impossible for knowledge and power. Does that view make God seem less personal to you? Would you feel comfortable praying to a God whose attributes are not really distinct?
?
If you were to choose between the two interpretations, which one would make you more certain that God is truly alive and responsive to you? How does that choice shape your trust in His attributes?

6 min
Proof from Repeated Perfect Actions
Razi presents the initial argument from repeated perfect actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how repeated perfect actions necessitate knowledge in their doer, and how this applies to God.

Core Idea
Repeated perfect acts prove knowledge; a rare perfect act does not.

Razi begins with a logical proof: actions that are consistently perfect cannot come from an ignorant agent. He illustrates with the analogy of handwriting and excludes intermediaries. This lays the foundation for affirming God's knowledge from the perfection of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how Razi uses the example of handwriting to illustrate the point.
The key is repeated perfection, not occasional perfection.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument has two parts: first, that repeated perfect acts cannot come from an ignorant being; second, that the creator of a perfect act from nothing must know its intricacies.
The second part is a direct proof for God.
3
Reflect on intermediaries
Razi briefly mentions that intermediaries have already been refuted. Consider why this is important: it prevents the objection that angels or nature produce perfect acts.
Intermediaries are irrelevant because they themselves are created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحقق حاكمة بأن أمثال ذلك لا يصدر عمن لا علم له ولا يتكرر ممن يقع منه فعل محكم مرة واحدة على سبيل الندرة وهو جاهل، ألا ترى أن من كتب مراراً خطا حسنا لا يمكن أن يتصور أنه أي جاهل بالخط، وأما الوسائط فقد تقدم إبطالها، وإيجاد من يفعل فعلا محكما من العدم فحيث يقدر على ذلك ويعلم دقائقه فعل في غاية الإحكام. وأما البحث عن معنى الإحكام والإتقان فالقول بأن الحكم بكون كل واحد يفعل فعلا محكما فهو عالم بديهي، وغير الموقوف على اكتساب تصور أجزائه يقتضي أن يكون تصور الحكم بديهيا. وأما أفعال الوسائط وأفعال الحيوانات فهي أفعال الله تعالى عند من يقول لا مؤثر إلا الله، وأما عند غيره فخلق مثل هذه الحيوانات محكم وإيجاد العلم فيها وإلهامها أحكم من إيجاد تلك الأفعال من غير توسطها. والمعارضة الأولى بكون العلم نسبة بين العالم والمعلوم والمقتضى لها ذاته وهى تقبلها فيكون الواحد فاعلا وقابلا، فالجواب عنها أن الإضافات لا توجد إلا في العقل، وهي تكون بين شيئين يقتضي كل واحد منهما صفة الإضافة في الآخر فيكون فاعلا وقابلا لشيء واحد، قوله يلزم من ذلك صدور أثر من شيء بسيط باطل، لأن القبول ليس بأثر ومن يفعل ويقبل لا يصدر عنه إلا أثر واحد، فإن حصول أثر غيره فيه لا يكون بأثر حصل منه. وجوابه عن قولهم: يشبه التأثير بالوجوب ويشبه القبول بالإمكان أن ذلك بالإمكان العام وهو لا ينافي الوجوب، ليس بصحيح، لأن مرادهم أن الفعل مع مؤثر يجب أن يوجد ومع قابله لا يجب وهذا الممكن بإزاء لا يجب فكيف يجتمع مع يجب. والمعارضة الثانية: بأن العلم كمال ولا يمكن أن الله تعالى يستفيد الكمال من غيره فليس جوابه أنه خطابي ولا يندفع بقوله إن العلم كمال والجهل نقصان وتعالى ذاته عن النقصان، فإن القائل يقول وتعالى الله عن الاستفادة كمالا عن غيره أيضا ونفى الاستفادة عن الله ليس بخطابي. والجواب أن الذوات الناقصة تستفيد الكمال من صفاتها الكاملة، أما الذوات الكاملة فصفاتها إنما تكون كاملة لكونها صفات لتلك الذوات وكمال العلم من هذا النوع، فإن سبب كماله كونه من صفات الله تعالى. فيكون ثبوتيا.
The investigator judges that such things do not proceed from one without knowledge, nor are repeated from one from whom a perfect act occurs once by way of rarity while he is ignorant. Do you not see that one who writes beautiful handwriting repeatedly cannot be imagined to be ignorant of handwriting? As for intermediaries, their refutation has already preceded. The creation of one who does a perfect act from nothing, so when he is able to do that and knows its intricacies, he does it in the utmost perfection. As for the inquiry into the meaning of mastery and precision: the statement that the judgment that every one who does a perfect act is knowledgeable is intuitive, and that which is not dependent on acquiring a conception of its parts requires that the conception of the judgment be intuitive. As for the acts of intermediaries and the acts of animals, they are acts of God according to those who say there is no effecter but God, and according to others, the creation of such animals is mastery and the creation of knowledge in them and their inspiration is more masterful than the creation of those acts without their mediation. The first objection: that knowledge is a relation between the knower and the known, and the essence demands it and accepts it, so the one becomes an agent and a recipient. The answer to it is that relations exist only in the mind, and they are between two things, each of which demands the attribute of relation in the other, so it is an agent and a recipient of one thing. His statement: from that follows the emanation of an effect from a simple thing is false, because reception is not an effect, and from one who acts and receives only one effect emanates. For the occurrence of another's effect in it is not an effect that came from him. His answer to their saying: 'He likens effecting to necessity and likens reception to possibility, that that is by general possibility which does not contradict necessity' is not correct, because they mean that the act with the effecter must exist, and with its recipient does not have to exist, and this possible in itself does not have to exist, so how does it coexist with 'must'? The second objection: that knowledge is perfection and it is not possible for God to acquire perfection from another; so his answer that it is rhetorical is not valid, nor is it repelled by his saying that knowledge is perfection and ignorance is deficiency and His essence is exalted above deficiency, for the objector says: Exalted is God also from acquiring perfection from another, and the negation of acquisition from God is not rhetorical. The answer is that deficient essences acquire perfection from their perfect attributes, but perfect essences, their attributes are only perfect because they are attributes of those essences, and the perfection of knowledge is of this type, for the cause of its perfection is its being an attribute of God. So it is positive.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and refutation. It begins with a proof that repeated perfect actions indicate knowledge: an investigator judges that such actions cannot come from an ignorant being, nor can they be repeated from someone who does a perfect act only rarely while ignorant; a parallel is drawn with handwriting. As for intermediaries, their role is dismissed. Then the meaning of 'mastery' (itqan) is discussed: the judgment that any doer of a perfect act is knowledgeable is intuitive. Acts of intermediaries and animals are ultimately from God. Then two objections are raised and answered. The first objection: knowledge is a relation requiring a recipient, so God would be both agent and recipient. The answer is that relations are mental, and each relatum requires the relation in the other, so it is possible to be both agent and recipient of one thing; reception is not an effect. A further objection about likening effect to necessity is refuted. The second objection: knowledge is perfection, but God cannot acquire perfection from another. The answer distinguishes deficient essences that gain perfection from attributes versus perfect essences whose attributes are perfect precisely because they belong to that essence; so God's knowledge is perfect because it is His attribute, and thus it is a positive (thubuti) attribute.

Key Terms
إحكام
إتقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key characteristic of the perfect action that proves knowledge in its doer?
A) It is a single, rare occurrence.
B) It is repeated and consistent.
C) It is performed by an intermediary.
D) It is done without any knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Repeated perfect actions necessarily imply knowledge in their agent, and the consistent perfection of creation points to God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness the repeated perfection of sunrise, seasons, and your own heartbeat, do you recognize them as signs of Allah's perfect knowledge, or do you take them for granted? What would change in your daily awareness if you truly internalized that each repeated perfect act demands a Knower? Commit to one moment today where you pause and say, 'This is from the One who knows all.'
?
Have you ever attributed a blessing in your life to 'luck' or 'coincidence' rather than to Allah's deliberate knowledge and plan? Razi says a rare perfect act could come from an ignorant being; do you treat some of Allah's gifts as if they were random? What does that reveal about your trust in His wisdom? Repent and resolve to attribute all good to His knowledge.
?
If you can create a single beautiful thing by chance (like a good grade or a kind word), but cannot sustain it, that might not prove deep knowledge. But Allah's creation is perfectly repeated every day. How does this challenge your own consistency in worship? Will you strive to make your good deeds habitual, reflecting knowledge, not just rare moments?

The Intuitive Judgment and Intermediaries
Razi explains the intuitive nature of the judgment and handles intermediaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the judgment linking perfect actions to knowledge is intuitive, and that intermediaries are ultimately dependent on God.

Core Idea
It is intuitive: whoever does a perfect act is knowledgeable.

Razi now clarifies that the connection between perfect action and knowledge is not acquired through reasoning but is immediate and self-evident. He then addresses the role of intermediaries and animals, showing that even if they perform perfect acts, it all traces back to God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the intuitive judgment
Read the passage on 'البحث عن معنى الإحكام والإتقان'. Notice that Razi says the judgment is 'بديهي' (intuitive).
This means it does not require proof; it is known instinctively.
2
Consider the role of intermediaries
Razi discusses two views: those who say only God acts (no effecter but God) and those who accept intermediaries. In both cases, the perfect acts ultimately belong to God.
Intermediaries themselves are created by God with knowledge and inspiration.
3
Reflect on the superiority of direct creation
Razi argues that creating animals with knowledge and inspiring them is even more perfect than creating the act directly without mediation.
This refutes any objection that intermediaries diminish God's involvement.

Source Text

Responding to the Objection about Knowledge as Relation
Razi refutes the objection that knowledge as a relation implies God is agent and recipient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that knowledge as a relation would make God both agent and recipient.

Core Idea
Relations exist only in the mind; they do not imply real recipienthood.

A significant objection is raised: if knowledge is a relation between knower and known, then God would be both agent (producing the relation) and recipient (receiving it). Razi responds by explaining that relations are mental constructs and do not entail real effect or reception.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first objection: knowledge is a relation (nisba) between knower and known. The essence demands that relation and also accepts it, making God both agent and recipient.
This objection tries to show a contradiction in God being simple yet having relational attributes.
2
Study Razi's answer
Razi replies that relations (idafat) exist only in the mind, not externally. Each relatum requires the relation in the other, so it is possible to be both agent and recipient of one thing.
The key point: reception (qabul) is not an effect (athar).
3
Examine the sub-argument
Razi rejects the objection that a simple being cannot be both agent and recipient. Reception is not an effect, so no multiplicity arises. He also addresses an objection about necessity and possibility.
The refutation of the necessity-possibility argument is more subtle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His answer to their saying: 'He likens effecting to necessity and likens reception to possibility, that that is by general possibility which does not contradict necessity' is not correct, because they mean that the act with the effecter must exist, and with its recipient does not have to exist, and this possible in itself does not have to exist, so how does it coexist with 'must'? The answer is that deficient essences acquire perfection from their perfect attributes, but perfect essences, their attributes are only perfect because they are attributes of those essences, and the perfection of knowledge is of this type, for the cause of its perfection is its being an attribute of God. So it is positive.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and refutation. It begins with a proof that repeated perfect actions indicate knowledge: an investigator judges that such actions cannot come from an ignorant being, nor can they be repeated from someone who does a perfect act only rarely while ignorant; a parallel is drawn with handwriting. As for intermediaries, their role is dismissed. Then the meaning of 'mastery' (itqan) is discussed: the judgment that any doer of a perfect act is knowledgeable is intuitive. Acts of intermediaries and animals are ultimately from God. Then two objections are raised and answered. The first objection: knowledge is a relation requiring a recipient, so God would be both agent and recipient. The answer is that relations are mental, and each relatum requires the relation in the other, so it is possible to be both agent and recipient of one thing; reception is not an effect. A further objection about likening effect to necessity is refuted. The second objection: knowledge is perfection, but God cannot acquire perfection from another. The answer distinguishes deficient essences that gain perfection from attributes versus perfect essences whose attributes are perfect precisely because they belong to that essence; so God's knowledge is perfect because it is His attribute, and thus it is a positive (thubuti) attribute.

إضافة

أثر
وجوب
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge being a relation implies God undergoes real change or reception.
Correction: Relations are mental constructs; they do not entail real change or multiplicity in God's essence.

Checkpoint
How does Razi explain that God can be both agent and recipient of the same relation?
A) By distinguishing between essence and attributes.
B) By saying relations exist only in the mind.
C) By denying that knowledge is a relation.
D) By claiming that God is not simple.

Key Takeaway
Relations are mental entities; they do not compromise God's simplicity or entail real reception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge of your sins, do you unconsciously feel that God is affected by them? How does Razi's argument that relations are mental reshape your understanding of God's transcendence? This week, whenever you confess your sins, remind yourself that God's knowledge does not change Him—He is perfectly unchanged and unaffected.
?
The objection assumed that being both agent and recipient is impossible for a simple being. In your own life, do you ever try to compartmentalize God as either the one who acts or the one who receives, forgetting His absolute simplicity? Commit this week to recite 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' (Nothing is like Him) with deeper contemplation before each prayer.
?
Razi insists that reception is not an effect. How does this distinction affect your understanding of God's relationship to creation? It means that nothing you do adds to or subtracts from His perfection. Are you living as if your actions truly matter to God in a way that changes Him, or are you living in humble awe of a God who is completely independent? Choose one area of your life where you act as if God needs your worship, and repent by worshipping Him as the Self-Sufficient.

Addressing the Objection about Acquiring Perfection
Razi refutes the objection that God cannot have knowledge because it would require acquiring perfection from another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge is perfect without being acquired from another, because it belongs to a perfect essence.

Core Idea
Perfect essences have perfect attributes; God's knowledge is perfect because it is His.

The second objection claims that if knowledge is perfection, God cannot acquire it from another, so either He lacks it or He acquires it. Razi's nuanced answer distinguishes between deficient and perfect essences: deficient essences gain perfection from attributes, but perfect essences are such that their attributes are perfect precisely because of their source.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second objection
Read the objection: knowledge is perfection (kmal), and God cannot acquire perfection from another, so how can He have knowledge?
The objector assumes that all perfection is acquired from outside.
2
Study Razi's initial refutation
Razi first says the answer is not just 'rhetorical' (khatabi); he rejects the idea that it's only a rhetorical argument. He insists on a substantive answer.
He then gives the real answer.
3
Grasp the distinction
Razi distinguishes between deficient essences that gain perfection from their attributes (like humans) and perfect essences whose attributes are perfect because they belong to that essence.
God's knowledge is perfect because it is His attribute, not because it gives Him perfection.
4
Conclusion: knowledge is positive
Thus God's knowledge is a positive (thubuti) attribute, not derived from elsewhere.
This affirms the reality of God's knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحقق حاكمة بأن أمثال ذلك لا يصدر عمن لا علم له ولا يتكرر ممن يقع منه فعل محكم مرة واحدة على سبيل الندرة وهو جاهل، ألا ترى أن من كتب مراراً خطا حسنا لا يمكن أن يتصور أنه أي جاهل بالخط، وأما الوسائط فقد تقدم إبطالها، وإيجاد من يفعل فعلا محكما من العدم فحيث يقدر على ذلك ويعلم دقائقه فعل في غاية الإحكام. وأما البحث عن معنى الإحكام والإتقان فالقول بأن الحكم بكون كل واحد يفعل فعلا محكما فهو عالم بديهي، وغير الموقوف على اكتساب تصور أجزائه يقتضي أن يكون تصور الحكم بديهيا. وأما أفعال الوسائط وأفعال الحيوانات فهي أفعال الله تعالى عند من يقول لا مؤثر إلا الله، وأما عند غيره فخلق مثل هذه الحيوانات محكم وإيجاد العلم فيها وإلهامها أحكم من إيجاد تلك الأفعال من غير توسطها. والمعارضة الأولى بكون العلم نسبة بين العالم والمعلوم والمقتضى لها ذاته وهى تقبلها فيكون الواحد فاعلا وقابلا، فالجواب عنها أن الإضافات لا توجد إلا في العقل، وهي تكون بين شيئين يقتضي كل واحد منهما صفة الإضافة في الآخر فيكون فاعلا وقابلا لشيء واحد، قوله يلزم من ذلك صدور أثر من شيء بسيط باطل، لأن القبول ليس بأثر ومن يفعل ويقبل لا يصدر عنه إلا أثر واحد، فإن حصول أثر غيره فيه لا يكون بأثر حصل منه. وجوابه عن قولهم: يشبه التأثير بالوجوب ويشبه القبول بالإمكان أن ذلك بالإمكان العام وهو لا ينافي الوجوب، ليس بصحيح، لأن مرادهم أن الفعل مع مؤثر يجب أن يوجد ومع قابله لا يجب وهذا الممكن بإزاء لا يجب فكيف يجتمع مع يجب. والمعارضة الثانية: بأن العلم كمال ولا يمكن أن الله تعالى يستفيد الكمال من غيره فليس جوابه أنه خطابي ولا يندفع بقوله إن العلم كمال والجهل نقصان وتعالى ذاته عن النقصان، فإن القائل يقول وتعالى الله عن الاستفادة كمالا عن غيره أيضا ونفى الاستفادة عن الله ليس بخطابي. والجواب أن الذوات الناقصة تستفيد الكمال من صفاتها الكاملة، أما الذوات الكاملة فصفاتها إنما تكون كاملة لكونها صفات لتلك الذوات وكمال العلم من هذا النوع، فإن سبب كماله كونه من صفات الله تعالى. فيكون ثبوتيا.
The investigator judges that such things do not proceed from one without knowledge, nor are repeated from one from whom a perfect act occurs once by way of rarity while he is ignorant. Do you not see that one who writes beautiful handwriting repeatedly cannot be imagined to be ignorant of handwriting? As for intermediaries, their refutation has already preceded. The creation of one who does a perfect act from nothing, so when he is able to do that and knows its intricacies, he does it in the utmost perfection. As for the inquiry into the meaning of mastery and precision: the statement that the judgment that every one who does a perfect act is knowledgeable is intuitive, and that which is not dependent on acquiring a conception of its parts requires that the conception of the judgment be intuitive. As for the acts of intermediaries and the acts of animals, they are acts of God according to those who say there is no effecter but God, and according to others, the creation of such animals is mastery and the creation of knowledge in them and their inspiration is more masterful than the creation of those acts without their mediation. The first objection: that knowledge is a relation between the knower and the known, and the essence demands it and accepts it, so the one becomes an agent and a recipient. The answer to it is that relations exist only in the mind, and they are between two things, each of which demands the attribute of relation in the other, so it is an agent and a recipient of one thing. His statement: from that follows the emanation of an effect from a simple thing is false, because reception is not an effect, and from one who acts and receives only one effect emanates. For the occurrence of another's effect in it is not an effect that came from him. His answer to their saying: 'He likens effecting to necessity and likens reception to possibility, that that is by general possibility which does not contradict necessity' is not correct, because they mean that the act with the effecter must exist, and with its recipient does not have to exist, and this possible in itself does not have to exist, so how does it coexist with 'must'? The second objection: that knowledge is perfection and it is not possible for God to acquire perfection from another; so his answer that it is rhetorical is not valid, nor is it repelled by his saying that knowledge is perfection and ignorance is deficiency and His essence is exalted above deficiency, for the objector says: Exalted is God also from acquiring perfection from another, and the negation of acquisition from God is not rhetorical. The answer is that deficient essences acquire perfection from their perfect attributes, but perfect essences, their attributes are only perfect because they are attributes of those essences, and the perfection of knowledge is of this type, for the cause of its perfection is its being an attribute of God. So it is positive.

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and refutation. It begins with a proof that repeated perfect actions indicate knowledge: an investigator judges that such actions cannot come from an ignorant being, nor can they be repeated from someone who does a perfect act only rarely while ignorant; a parallel is drawn with handwriting. Acts of intermediaries and animals are ultimately from God. Then two objections are raised and answered. The first objection: knowledge is a relation requiring a recipient, so God would be both agent and recipient. The answer is that relations are mental, and each relatum requires the relation in the other, so it is possible to be both agent and recipient of one thing; reception is not an effect. A further objection about likening effect to necessity is refuted. The second objection: knowledge is perfection, but God cannot acquire perfection from another. Inconsistency in Razi's Argument on Life as Positive
Marginal note criticising Razi's argument on life as positive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction in Razi's argument about life being a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Razi's own statements on possibility contradict his argument for life being positive.

This brief marginal note exposes a tension in Imam Razi's theological reasoning. Razi had argued that since impossibility is non-existent, its negation (i.e., life) must be positive. But he elsewhere insisted that possibility, the opposite of impossibility, is not positive. The note shows that these two positions cannot both be true.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the gist. Then read the literal translation.
Notice the key terms: صفة سلبية, صفة ثبوتية, امتناع, إمكان.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage mentions two theological positions: those who hold that attributes are additional to God's essence (so life is positive) and those who do not (so life is negative).
This sets the context for Razi's argument.
3
Pinpoint the contradiction
Razi's stronger argument treats the negation of impossibility as positive, but his repeated claim that possibility (the opposite of impossibility) is not positive creates a direct contradiction.
Ask yourself: If negation of impossibility is positive, why isn't its opposite (possibility) also positive?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول الذين يذهبون إلى أن الصفات يجوز أن تكون زائدة على ذاته تعالى يذهبون إلى أن الحياة صفة زائدة، والذين لا يجوزون ذلك يجعلونها سلبية، وما جعله المصنف أقوى وهو أن الامتناع عدمي فعدمه ثبوتي مناقض لما ذكره مرارا من أن الإمكان الذي هو نقيض الامتناع ليس بثبوت
I say: Those who hold that attributes may be additional to His essence hold that life is an additional attribute, and those who do not allow that make it negative. What the author made stronger—that impossibility is non-existent so its negation is positive—contradicts what he mentioned repeatedly, that possibility, which is the opposite of impossibility, is not positive.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a critical note (likely from a commentator or editor) pointing out an inconsistency in Imam Razi's own argument. Razi had argued that impossibility is non-existent (adami), so its negation (i.e., life) would be the negation of non-existence, which is a negation of negation. But he also repeatedly stated that possibility (imkan), which is the opposite of impossibility, is not positive (thubuti). The note claims that this stance contradicts his weaker argument for life being positive, because if the negation of impossibility is positive, then possibility (its opposite) would also be positive, which Razi denies. This highlights a tension in Razi's system regarding the ontological status of privations.

Key Terms
صفة سلبية
صفة ثبوتية
امتناع
إمكان
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the negation of non-existence is always positive, regardless of context.
Correction: The note shows that Razi's own system treats possibility as non-positive, so the negation of impossibility cannot be straightforwardly positive without contradiction.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the marginal note identify in Razi's argument?
A) He says impossibility is positive but also says possibility is non-existent.
B) He says the negation of impossibility is positive, but he also says possibility (its opposite) is not positive.
C) He defines life as an action rather than an attribute.
D) He denies that attributes can be positive.

Key Takeaway
A contradictory pair of statements in Razi's works undermines his stronger argument for life as a positive attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one belief you hold about Allah's attributes that you have never questioned? Are you willing to examine it for internal consistency, even if it makes you uncomfortable?
?
Razi's inconsistency was exposed by his own words. When was the last time you courageously admitted that your own reasoning about a religious matter was flawed? What would it take for you to revisit your assumptions today?
?
This passage shows that even great scholars can contradict themselves. Are you open to the possibility that some of your deeply held views might be based on inconsistent reasoning? Will you seek clarity, even if it means changing your position?

The Dispute over the Meaning of Divine Will
Razi enumerates the views on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main positions on the meaning of God's will among Muslim theologians.

Core Idea
Muslims agree God wills, but disagree on what 'will' means.

This lesson introduces the point of consensus and the points of disagreement regarding the attribute of divine will. Before we can prove that will is an additional attribute, we must first see what other scholars have said about its meaning. The source passage lists four distinct views, and understanding them sets the stage for the Ash'ari proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus
Read the first sentence: Muslims agree that God is willing (mureed). Notice that the disagreement is not about whether He wills, but about the meaning of that will.
Consensus is important in kalam to establish the shared starting point.
2
List the four views
Find the four different interpretations: Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's knowledge-of-benefit view, al-Bukhari's non-coercion view, al-Ka'bi's two-part view, and the Ash'ari/Mu'tazili view of an additional attribute.
Al-Ka'bi distinguishes between God's own acts and the acts of others.
3
Notice who holds what
Identify that the last view (additional attribute) is held by 'us' (Ash'aris) and by Abu Ali and Abu Hashim (Mu'tazila). This shows agreement across schools on this point.
This sets up the proof that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اتفق المسلمون على أنه تعالى مريد لكنهم اختلفوا في معناه. فذهب أبو الحسين البصرى إلى أن معناه علمه بما في الفعل من المصلحة الداعية إلى الإيجاد، ومن البخاري أن معناه أنه غير مغلوب ولا مستكره، وعن الكي أن معناه في أفعال نفسه كونه عالما بها وفي أفعال غيره كونه آمرا بها، وعندنا وعند أبي علي وأبي هاشم صفة زائدة على العلم. لنا أن حصول أفعاله تعالى في أوقات معينة مع جواز حصولها قبلها أو بعدها يستدعي مخصصا وليس هو القدرة لأن شأنها الإيجاد الذي نسبته إلى كل الأوقات على السواء، ولا العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم فلا يكون مستتبعا له لامتناع الدور، وظاهر أن سائر الصفات لا يصلح لذلك سوى الإرادة فلابد من إثباتها. فإن قيل لا نسلم جواز حصول الفعل قبل أن حصل وبعده، ولم لا يجوز أن يقال لا إمكان له إلا في ذلك الزمان المعين، والدليل عليه وهو أن المفهوم من حصوله في ذلك الزمان وجب أن يبطل الذات فهو إذا صفة زائدة على الذات، لكن هذه الصفة يستحيل حصولها إلا في ذلك الزمان، فإذا إمكان حدوث هذه الصفة مختص بهذا الوقت فإذا عقل هنا فلم لا يعقل في غيره. فإن قلت الإمكان من لوازم الماهية فيدوم بدوامها. قلت ينتقض بما ذكرنا، ثم نقول هذا إنما يصح لو كانت الماهية متقررة قبل وجودها لكن ذلك باطل، لأنه بناء على أن الماهية متقررة حال عدمها وهو قول بأن المعدوم شيء وهو باطل، سلمنا ذلك لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال الماهية بشرط حصولها في هذا الوقت يقتضي الإمكان وشرط حصولها في وقت آخر يقتضي الامتناع، كما أن الطبيعة الأرضية بشرط حصولها في المركز يقتضي السكون وبشرط حصولها في الهواء يقتضي الحركة، سلمنا الإمكان، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال الله تعالى خلق الأفلاك وخلق فيها طباعا محركة لها لذواتها، ثم إن بسببها تتولد هذه الحوادث في عالمنا هذا وإذا كانت الحوادث العنصرية مرتبطة بالاتصالات الفلكية لها مناهج معينة يمتنع فيها تقدم المتأخر وتأخر المتقدم كانت الحوادث العنصرية كذلك وحينئذ لا حاجة بها إلى المخصص. فإن قلت فلم خلق العالم في الوقت المعين، وما خلقه قبل ذلك ولا بعد. قلت هذا إنما يصبح لو كان قبل خلق الفلك وقت وزمان وذلك محال بالاتفاق، أما عند الفلاسفة فلأن الزمان مقدار حركة معدل النهار فقبل وجودها لا يمكن وجود الزمان، وأما عند المسلمين فلأن الزمان محدث، وإذا كان كذلك فقبل الخلق لا زمان فيستحيل أن يقال لم لم يخلقه في زمان آخر، سلمنا أنه لا بد من مخصص فلم لا يكفي القدرة. قوله: نسبتها إلى الكل على السواء.
Problem: The Muslims agreed that He, exalted, is willing, but they differed on its meaning. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri held that its meaning is His knowledge of the benefit in the act that calls for bringing it into existence. Al-Bukhari held that its meaning is that He is not overcome nor coerced. Al-Ka'bi held that its meaning regarding His own acts is that He is knowing of them, and regarding the acts of others is that He commands them. According to us and to Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, it is an attribute additional to knowledge. Our proof: The occurrence of His acts at specific times, despite the possibility of their occurring before or after, requires a specifier. It is not power, because power's function is bringing into existence with equal relation to all times. Nor is it knowledge, because knowledge follows the known, so it cannot be a cause for it due to the impossibility of circularity. It is evident that none of the other attributes are suitable for this except will, so it must be affirmed. If it is said: We do not concede the possibility of the act before it occurred or after; why may it not be said that there is no possibility for it except in that particular time? The evidence for it is that the concept of its occurrence in that time necessitates that the essence be in a state, so it is an attribute additional to the essence, but this attribute cannot occur except in that time. So if the possibility of this attribute's occurrence is specific to this time, then if it is understood here, why not in other cases? If you say: Possibility is among the concomitants of the essence, so it persists with its persistence. I say: It is rebutted by what we mentioned. Then we say: This holds only if the essence were determined before its existence, but that is false, because it is based on the claim that the essence is determined in the state of non-existence, which is the statement that the non-existent is a thing, and that is false. We concede that, but why may it not be said: the essence with the condition of occurring in this time entails possibility, and with the condition of occurring in another time entails impossibility, just as the earthly nature with the condition of being at the center entails rest, and with the condition of being in the air entails motion. We concede possibility, so why may it not be said that God created the spheres and created in them moving natures for themselves, then through them these events in our world are generated? And if elemental events are connected to astral conjunctions with fixed courses in which it is impossible for the later to precede and the earlier to be delayed, then elemental events are likewise, and then there is no need for a specifier. If you say: Then why did He create the world at that specific time, and not before or after? I say: This holds only if there were time before the creation of the sphere, but that is impossible by consensus. According to the philosophers, time is the measure of the motion of the celestial sphere, so before its existence time cannot exist. According to Muslims, time is created. If so, before creation there is no time, so one cannot ask why He did not create it at another time. We concede that a specifier is necessary, so why is power not sufficient? His statement: Its relation to all times is equal.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a full theological problem on divine will (irada). Imam Razi begins by noting Muslim consensus that God is willing, but disagreement on its meaning. He lists four views: (1) Abu al-Husayn al-Basri: knowledge of benefit motivating creation. (2) Al-Bukhari (likely Abu Abdullah al-Bukhari?): not being overcome or coerced. (3) Al-Ka'bi: regarding His own acts, knowledge; regarding others' acts, command. (4) The Ash'ari and Mu'tazila (Abu Ali and Abu Hashim) view: an additional attribute beyond knowledge. Then Razi presents the proof from our school: The fact that God's acts occur at specific times, even though they could have occurred earlier or later, requires a specifier (mukhassis). Power cannot be the specifier because power relates equally to all times. Knowledge cannot be the specifier because knowledge follows the known (it cannot cause it due to circularity). So will must be affirmed. Then a lengthy objection is raised: One might deny that the act had temporal possibility before or after; perhaps its possibility is confined to that specific time. The objection elaborates that if the act's occurrence requires an attribute that can only exist at that time, then possibility is specific. The response counters that this would require the essence to be determined before existence, which is invalid (since non-existent is not a thing). Then a further objection uses the example of the essence conditioned to a time entailing possibility or impossibility, like earth's nature. Then another objection invokes astral determinism: if events are necessitated by celestial motions, no specifier is needed. The answer is that before creation there was no time (philosophers say time is measure of motion; Muslims say time is created), so the question of a different time is meaningless. Finally, it is conceded that a specifier is needed, but why not power? The reply (quoted) is that power relates equally to all times, so it cannot specify.

Key Terms
إرادة
مريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consensus among Muslims regarding God?
A) God is powerful.
B) God is willing.
C) God is knowing.
D) God is the creator.

Key Takeaway
While all Muslims affirm that God wills, theologians differ on whether will is reducible to knowledge or is an additional attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars differed on what it means for God to be 'willing'. Which of these views do you instinctively lean towards? Does your view make you feel more in control of your destiny, or more dependent on Him? Examine your heart: is your theology shaped by your desire for autonomy or by submission to revelation?
?
Al-Ka'bi said that regarding others' acts, God's will means He commands them. When you read a command in the Quran, do you treat it as a mere suggestion or as the actual will of God for you? What one command have you been ignoring that you need to obey today?
?
The view that will is additional to knowledge means God's will is not just passive awareness but active choice. In your daily life, do you live as if God's will is a passive 'knowing' or an active 'choosing'? How would your prayers change if you truly believed that His will is a distinct, powerful force that can intervene?

The Proof for Will as an Additional Attribute
Razi presents the proof that will is an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the temporal specification of God's acts proves the necessity of a distinct attribute called will.

Core Idea
Specific times for creation require a specifier that power and knowledge cannot be.

After presenting the different views on will, Razi now gives the Ash'ari proof that will must be an attribute additional to knowledge and power. The argument hinges on the fact that God's acts occur at specific times despite being possible earlier or later. This temporal specification cannot be explained by power or knowledge, so an attribute called will must be affirmed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fact to be explained
Recognize that God's acts happen at particular times, even though they could have occurred earlier or later. That fact needs a cause or specifier.
This is the starting point of the proof.
2
Examine why power is insufficient
Power relates equally to all times, so it cannot prefer one moment over another. Therefore power cannot be the specifier.
Razi says 'because power's function is bringing into existence with equal relation to all times.'
3
Examine why knowledge is insufficient
Knowledge follows the known and cannot cause it, otherwise there would be circularity. So knowledge cannot be the specifier either.
The argument is that the known (the act) would have to exist before knowledge causes it, leading to a regress.
4
Conclude to will
Since no other attribute suffices, we must affirm a distinct attribute called will that determines the specific time.
This establishes that will is additional to knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اتفق المسلمون على أنه تعالى مريد لكنهم اختلفوا في معناه. فذهب أبو الحسين البصرى إلى أن معناه علمه بما في الفعل من المصلحة الداعية إلى الإيجاد، ومن البخاري أن معناه أنه غير مغلوب ولا مستكره، وعن الكي أن معناه في أفعال نفسه كونه عالما بها وفي أفعال غيره كونه آمرا بها، وعندنا وعند أبي علي وأبي هاشم صفة زائدة على العلم. لنا أن حصول أفعاله تعالى في أوقات معينة مع جواز حصولها قبلها أو بعدها يستدعي مخصصا وليس هو القدرة لأن شأنها الإيجاد الذي نسبته إلى كل الأوقات على السواء، ولا العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم فلا يكون مستتبعا له لامتناع الدور، وظاهر أن سائر الصفات لا يصلح لذلك سوى الإرادة فلابد من إثباتها. فإن قيل لا نسلم جواز حصول الفعل قبل أن حصل وبعده، ولم لا يجوز أن يقال لا إمكان له إلا في ذلك الزمان المعين، والدليل عليه وهو أن المفهوم من حصوله في ذلك الزمان وجب أن يبطل الذات فهو إذا صفة زائدة على الذات، لكن هذه الصفة يستحيل حصولها إلا في ذلك الزمان، فإذا إمكان حدوث هذه الصفة مختص بهذا الوقت فإذا عقل هنا فلم لا يعقل في غيره. فإن قلت الإمكان من لوازم الماهية فيدوم بدوامها. قلت ينتقض بما ذكرنا، ثم نقول هذا إنما يصح لو كانت الماهية متقررة قبل وجودها لكن ذلك باطل، لأنه بناء على أن الماهية متقررة حال عدمها وهو قول بأن المعدوم شيء وهو باطل، سلمنا ذلك لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال الماهية بشرط حصولها في هذا الوقت يقتضي الإمكان وشرط حصولها في وقت آخر يقتضي الامتناع، كما أن الطبيعة الأرضية بشرط حصولها في المركز يقتضي السكون وبشرط حصولها في الهواء يقتضي الحركة، سلمنا الإمكان، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال الله تعالى خلق الأفلاك وخلق فيها طباعا محركة لها لذواتها، ثم إن بسببها تتولد هذه الحوادث في عالمنا هذا وإذا كانت الحوادث العنصرية مرتبطة بالاتصالات الفلكية لها مناهج معينة يمتنع فيها تقدم المتأخر وتأخر المتقدم كانت الحوادث العنصرية كذلك وحينئذ لا حاجة بها إلى المخصص. فإن قلت فلم خلق العالم في الوقت المعين، وما خلقه قبل ذلك ولا بعد. قلت هذا إنما يصبح لو كان قبل خلق الفلك وقت وزمان وذلك محال بالاتفاق، أما عند الفلاسفة فلأن الزمان مقدار حركة معدل النهار فقبل وجودها لا يمكن وجود الزمان، وأما عند المسلمين فلأن الزمان محدث، وإذا كان كذلك فقبل الخلق لا زمان فيستحيل أن يقال لم لم يخلقه في زمان آخر، سلمنا أنه لا بد من مخصص فلم لا يكفي القدرة. قوله: نسبتها إلى الكل على السواء.
Problem: The Muslims agreed that He, exalted, is willing, but they differed on its meaning. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri held that its meaning is His knowledge of the benefit in the act that calls for bringing it into existence. Al-Bukhari held that its meaning is that He is not overcome nor coerced. Al-Ka'bi held that its meaning regarding His own acts is that He is knowing of them, and regarding the acts of others is that He commands them. According to us and to Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, it is an attribute additional to knowledge. Our proof: The occurrence of His acts at specific times, despite the possibility of their occurring before or after, requires a specifier. It is not power, because power's function is bringing into existence with equal relation to all times. Nor is it knowledge, because knowledge follows the known, so it cannot be a cause for it due to the impossibility of circularity. It is evident that none of the other attributes are suitable for this except will, so it must be affirmed. If it is said: We do not concede the possibility of the act before it occurred or after; why may it not be said that there is no possibility for it except in that particular time? The evidence for it is that the concept of its occurrence in that time necessitates that the essence be in a state, so it is an attribute additional to the essence, but this attribute cannot occur except in that time. So if the possibility of this attribute's occurrence is specific to this time, then if it is understood here, why not in other cases? If you say: Possibility is among the concomitants of the essence, so it persists with its persistence. I say: It is rebutted by what we mentioned. Then we say: This holds only if the essence were determined before its existence, but that is false, because it is based on the claim that the essence is determined in the state of non-existence, which is the statement that the non-existent is a thing, and that is false. We concede that, but why may it not be said: the essence with the condition of occurring in this time entails possibility, and with the condition of occurring in another time entails impossibility, just as the earthly nature with the condition of being at the center entails rest, and with the condition of being in the air entails motion. We concede possibility, so why may it not be said that God created the spheres and created in them moving natures for themselves, then through them these events in our world are generated? And if elemental events are connected to astral conjunctions with fixed courses in which it is impossible for the later to precede and the earlier to be delayed, then elemental events are likewise, and then there is no need for a specifier. If you say: Then why did He create the world at that specific time, and not before or after? I say: This holds only if there were time before the creation of the sphere, but that is impossible by consensus. According to the philosophers, time is the measure of the motion of the celestial sphere, so before its existence time cannot exist. According to Muslims, time is created. If so, before creation there is no time, so one cannot ask why He did not create it at another time. We concede that a specifier is necessary, so why is power not sufficient? His statement: Its relation to all times is equal.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a full theological problem on divine will (irada). Imam Razi begins by noting Muslim consensus that God is willing, but disagreement on its meaning. He lists four views: (1) Abu al-Husayn al-Basri: knowledge of benefit motivating creation. (2) Al-Bukhari (likely Abu Abdullah al-Bukhari?): not being overcome or coerced. (3) Al-Ka'bi: regarding His own acts, knowledge; regarding others' acts, command. (4) The Ash'ari and Mu'tazila (Abu Ali and Abu Hashim) view: an additional attribute beyond knowledge. Then Razi presents the proof from our school: The fact that God's acts occur at specific times, even though they could have occurred earlier or later, requires a specifier (mukhassis). Power cannot be the specifier because power relates equally to all times. Knowledge cannot be the specifier because knowledge follows the known (it cannot cause it due to circularity). So will must be affirmed. Then a lengthy objection is raised: One might deny that the act had temporal possibility before or after; perhaps its possibility is confined to that specific time. The objection elaborates that if the act's occurrence requires an attribute that can only exist at that time, then possibility is specific. The response counters that this would require the essence to be determined before existence, which is invalid (since non-existent is not a thing). Then a further objection uses the example of the essence conditioned to a time entailing possibility or impossibility, like earth's nature. Then another objection invokes astral determinism: if events are necessitated by celestial motions, no specifier is needed. The answer is that before creation there was no time (philosophers say time is measure of motion; Muslims say time is created), so the question of a different time is meaningless. Finally, it is conceded that a specifier is needed, but why not power? The reply (quoted) is that power relates equally to all times, so it cannot specify.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power alone can explain why God creates at specific times.
Correction: Razi argues power's relation to all times is equal, so it cannot prefer one time over another. A specifying attribute (will) is needed.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that knowledge cannot be the specifier of God's acts?
A) Knowledge is eternal and unchanging.
B) Knowledge is dependent on the known and cannot cause it.
C) Knowledge is a temporal attribute.
D) Knowledge is shared by all beings.

Key Takeaway
The temporal specificity of God's acts proves that will is an attribute distinct from power and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment in your life that happened at a specific time you could not have planned or expected. If you believe it was God's will alone that determined that timing, how does that change your trust in God's plan over your own? Will you consciously rely more on His will for your future decisions?
?
The proof shows that power alone cannot prefer one time over another. When you think about your own abilities and plans, are you tempted to believe that your power or knowledge can guarantee outcomes? How can you remind yourself daily that only God's will ultimately specifies what happens and when?
?
If God's knowledge simply follows what He will create, but His will actively chooses, how does that affect your humility? Are you willing to accept that you do not know the 'best time' for things? This week, try to surrender one specific desire or deadline to God's will and observe what happens.

Responding to Objections on Divine Will
Razi responds to several objections to his proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main objections raised against the necessity of a specifier for God's acts and the Ash'ari responses to them.

Core Idea
Objections to the proof for will are met by denying pre-temporal possibility, refuting essentialist views, and eliminating the need for a specifier via astral determinism, all rebutted by Razi.

Razi anticipates several objections to his proof that will is an additional attribute. The objections challenge the assumption that the act could have occurred earlier or later, the nature of possibility, and even suggest that celestial motions make a specifier unnecessary. Razi answers each one, refining the doctrine of divine will. This lesson will walk through these objections and responses as presented in the passage.

Guided Path
1
First objection: No possibility before/after
The opponent denies that the act could have occurred earlier or later; perhaps its possibility is confined to that time. Read the objection and Razi's reply that this would require the essence to be determined before existence, which is false.
This objection ties to the debate about the status of non-existent things.
2
Second objection: Essence-conditioned possibility
The opponent analogizes with earthly nature: just as earth rests at the center and moves in the air, the essence may entail possibility only in a specific time. Razi concedes possibility but then raises the astral determinism objection.
This is a sophisticated essentialist argument.
3
Third objection: Astral determinism
The opponent argues that if events are necessitated by celestial motions, no specifier is needed. Razi responds that before creation there was no time, so the question of 'why then?' is invalid.
This objection draws from Aristotelian cosmology.
4
Final concession: Why not power?
The opponent concedes a specifier is needed but suggests power suffices. Razi quotes his earlier statement that power's relation to all times is equal.
This reinforces the main proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Razi refutes the notion that essences have any reality apart from God's will. Do you ever treat your own identity, talents, or status as something you own, rather than as a gift that God creates anew each instant? Commit to thanking Him for your very being as a fresh creation.

The Ash'ari Proof for Will as a Distinct Attribute
Razi presents the Ash'ari argument for will as a distinct attribute based on temporal specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that Allah's will is a distinct attribute, based on the need for a specificator of temporal events.

Core Idea
Will alone can specify the time of God's acts, not power or knowledge.

Imam al-Razi presents a concise proof for the reality of will as a separate divine attribute. He begins with a premise: God's acts occur at specific times, though they could have occurred at other times. This requires something that 'specifies' the actual time. By eliminating other candidates, he concludes that will must be that specificator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice that Razi uses a logical elimination method.
2
Identify the premise
The argument starts with the fact that Allah's acts happen at specific times despite being possible at other times. This requires a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ).
The premise is based on the contingency of timing.
3
Eliminate power and knowledge
Razi argues that power (qudra) relates equally to all times, so it cannot specify. Knowledge ('ilm) follows the known, so it would cause circularity (dawr).
Understand why each is disqualified.
4
Affirm will
Since no other attribute can serve as specificator, will (irāda) must be a distinct attribute that performs this role.
This establishes will as separate from power and knowledge.
5
Reflect on the argument's structure
Consider why this argument is foundational in Ash'ari theology and how it differs from other theological schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن حصول أفعاله تعالى في أوقات معينة مع جواز حصولها قبلها أو بعدها يستدعى مخصصا وليس هو القدرة لأن شأنها الإيجاد الذى نسبته إلى كل الأوقات على السواء ، ولا العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم فلا يكون [مستقلا؟] به لامتناع الدور ، وظاهر أن سائر الصفات لا يصلح لذلك سوى الإرادة فلابد من إثباتها
We [Ash'aris] [argue that] the occurrence of His acts, exalted, at specific times, despite the possibility of their occurring before or after, requires a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ). And it is not power (qudra), because its function is bringing into existence, whose relation to all times is equal. Nor is it knowledge ('ilm), because it follows the known, so it cannot be [independent?] of it due to the impossibility of circularity. And it is apparent that the other attributes are not suitable for that except will (irada), so it must be affirmed.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) for the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah's will is a distinct attribute, separate from power and knowledge. The argument is based on the need for a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ) of God's acts at particular times. Since His acts could have occurred earlier or later, there must be something that specifies the actual time. This specificator cannot be power, because power relates equally to all times. It cannot be knowledge, because knowledge follows the known; it would be circular for knowledge to determine the known. None of the other attributes (like life, speech, etc.) can serve this role. Therefore, will must be that specificator, proving it as an additional attribute. Razi follows the classical Ash'ari argument from temporal particularization (takhṣīṣ) to establish a distinct attribute of will.

Key Terms
إرادة
مخصص
قدرة
علم
دور
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that power alone is sufficient to explain why an act occurs at a specific time.
Correction: Razi shows that power relates equally to all times, so it cannot be the specificator. Another attribute—will—is needed to determine the particular time.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that knowledge cannot be the specificator of the time of an act?
A) Because knowledge is not a real attribute
B) Because knowledge follows the known, leading to circularity
C) Because knowledge is eternal and cannot change
D) Because knowledge is the same as will

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari use temporal particularization to prove that will is a distinct, irreducible divine attribute separate from power and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life unfolds at specific moments—your birth, your faith, your trials. Do you truly believe that each of these was appointed by God's will, not just His power or knowledge? If so, why do you sometimes resent the timing of events, as if they were random?
?
When you pray for something and it comes late or not at all, do you attribute that to God's will specifically choosing that time or absence? Or do you secretly doubt that His will is at work? How would your patience change if you fully internalized that every delay is a deliberate act of will?
?
The argument proves that will is distinct from power and knowledge. If God's will is not reducible to what He can do or what He knows, then your relationship with Him must involve submission to His choices, not just acknowledgment of His ability. Are you living in surrender to His will, or merely convinced of His power?

Objection on Time-Specific Possibility and Razi's Response
Razi presents an objection and response on time-specific possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine acts are only possible at the time they occur, and Razi's philosophical refutation based on the nature of quiddity and possibility.

Core Idea
Possibility is not tied to time; it is a necessary concomitant of quiddity.

In this passage, Razi presents a dialectical exchange. An objector argues that an act's possibility is exclusive to the specific time of its occurrence, using a proof based on attributes. Razi responds by invoking the principle that possibility is a concomitant of the quiddity, which persists. The objector then refines his claim with an analogy from natural phenomena, challenging Razi to explain why possibility cannot be time-relative.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully.
Note the objector's claim: possibility is only at that specific time. Identify his proof: the occurrence implies an attribute that invalidates the essence.
2
Examine Razi's first response.
Razi says possibility is a concomitant of the quiddity, so it should persist. Think about what this implies about the relationship between possibility and existence.
3
Analyze the counter-objection with analogy.
The objector uses the analogy of earthly nature to argue that conditions can change possibility. Consider the implications of this analogy for Razi's position.
4
Reflect on the underlying issue.
The debate hinges on whether the quiddity exists independently of time. Razi rejects that, as it leads to affirming non-existence as a thing.
This connects to broader kalam debates about the status of non-existent entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل لا نسلم جواز حصول الله تعالى قبل أن حصل وبعده ، ولم لا يجوز أن يقال لا إمكان لها إلا في ذلك الزمان المعين، والدليل عليه وهو أن المفهوم من حصوله في ذلك الزمان وجب أن يبطل الذات فهو إذا صفة زائدة على الذات، لكن هذه الصفة يستحيل حصولها إلا في ذلك الزمان ، فإذا إمكان حدوث هذه الصفة مختص بهذا الوقت فإذا عقل هنا فلم لا يعقل في غيره . فإن قلت الإمكان من لوازم الماهية فيدوم بدوامها . قلت ينتقض بما ذكرنا ، ثم نقول هذا إنما يصح لو كانت الماهية متقررة قبل وجودها لكن ذلك باطل ، لأنه بناء على أن الماهية متقررة حال عدمها وهو قول بأن المعدوم شيء وهو باطل ، سلمنا ذلك لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال الماهية بشرط حصولها في هذا الوقت يقتضى الإمكان وشرط حصولها في وقت آخر يقتضى الامتناع ، كما أن الطبيعة الأرضية بشرط حصولها في المركز يقتضى السكون وبشرط حصولها في الهواء يقتضى الحركة
For it is said: We do not grant the possibility of [the act] occurring before or after it occurred. And why is it not permissible to say that it has no possibility except in that specific time? The proof of that is that what is understood from its occurrence at that time necessitates the invalidation of the essence, so it is an attribute additional to the essence. But this attribute cannot occur except at that time. So if the possibility of the occurrence of this attribute is specific to this time, and it is conceivable here, why is it not conceivable in other times? Then you say: Possibility is a concomitant of the quiddity, so it endures as long as it endures. I say: This is refuted by what we mentioned. Then we say: This is only valid if the quiddity were established before its existence, but that is false, because it is based on the quiddity being established in the state of its non-existence, which is the statement that the non-existent is a thing, and that is false. Granting that, but why is it not permissible to say that the quiddity, on condition of its occurrence at this time, necessitates possibility, and on condition of its occurrence at another time necessitates impossibility, just as the earthly nature, on condition of being at the center, necessitates rest, and on condition of being in the air, necessitates motion?
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is an objection (i'tirad) to the Ash'ari proof and the beginning of the response. The objector denies that an act could have been possible before or after its actual time, suggesting that possibility is tied only to that specific time. He argues that the occurrence itself implies an attribute that necessitates that time, so possibility is not general. The responder (Razi) first counters that possibility is a concomitant (lazim) of the quiddity (mahiyya) and thus should persist. The objector then modifies his claim: the quiddity with the condition of being at that time entails possibility, while at another time it entails impossibility, analogizing to the earthly nature which necessitates rest at the center and motion in the air. Razi's response is cut off here, but the argument continues. This back-and-forth shows the dialectical nature of kalam reasoning.

Key Terms
إمكان
ماهية
صفة زائدة
امتناع
طبيعة أرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that possibility (imkan) is a real attribute that can inhere in a non-existent thing or be time-bound.
Correction: Razi refutes this by showing that possibility is a concomitant of the quiddity, not an attribute that can be attached to non-existence. The objector's analogy is flawed because quiddity is not pre-existent.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the objector use to argue that a thing can be possible at one time and impossible at another?
A) The analogy of water turning to ice
B) The analogy of earthly nature at the center vs. in the air
C) The analogy of fire burning different materials
D) The analogy of human will changing with time

Key Takeaway
The objection that possibility is time-specific is refuted by showing that possibility is a concomitant of the quiddity, not an attribute that can be time-bound, and the analogy with earthly nature is undermined by the fact that quiddity does not pre-exist its actualization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector argues that the possibility of an act is confined to its time. In your own life, do you ever assume that certain possibilities are only available at certain times, thereby limiting your trust in Allah's ability to bring things about at any time? What verse or hadith comes to mind that affirms Allah's absolute power over time? Reflect on a specific situation where you felt a door was closed forever, and ask Allah to expand your certainty in His boundless ability.
?
Razi insists that possibility is a concomitant of quiddity, not time-bound. How does this truth affect your certainty that Allah knows all possibilities, even those that never occur? Can you recall a moment when you doubted Allah's comprehensive knowledge—perhaps when a prayer seemed unanswered? Commit to strengthening your conviction that nothing is outside His knowledge or power.
?
The analogy of earthly nature suggests that conditions determine possibility. What 'conditions' in your life—such as age, wealth, or social status—might be falsely convincing you that something is impossible, when in reality the quiddity of that thing is possible with Allah? Are you allowing worldly constraints to overshadow divine ability? Write down one 'impossible' situation you face, and pray with the understanding that possibility is inherent in its essence, not its circumstances.

The Objection from Celestial Motions and the Response on Time
Razi presents an objection from celestial spheres and responds by denying pre-creation time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that celestial motions could determine events, eliminating the need for a divine specificator, by arguing that time itself is created.

Core Idea
Time did not exist before creation, so the question of 'why this time?' is meaningless.

Razi presents a sophisticated objection from natural causation, then answers by showing that the very framework of time is a created entity. This lesson examines that exchange and clarifies the Islamic stance on time.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection from 'سلمنا الإمكان' to 'لا حاجة بها إلى المخصص'. Understand how the objector argues that if events follow from celestial natures and conjunctions, no specificator is needed.
Notice the conditional: if we grant possibility, then why not this explanation?
2
Understand the specificator argument
Reflect on why the objector thinks celestial motions suffice. The key is that the patterns are fixed (later cannot precede, etc.), so events are determined by these patterns.
3
Grasp the response on time
Read Razi's reply from 'فإن قلت' onward. He counters by asking why the world was created at a specific moment, then shows that before creation there was no time, so the question is invalid.
Razi cites both philosophers and Muslims to show agreement that time does not pre-exist creation.
4
See how this defends divine will
Consider how this response preserves the need for a specificator: the specific moment of creation is not determined by any prior condition, hence it must be by divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا الإمكان ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال الله تعالى خلق الافلاك وخلق فيها طباعا محركة لها لذواتها ، ثم إن بسببها تتولد هذه الحوادث في عالمنا هذا وإذا كانت الحوادث العنصرية مرتبطة بالاتصالات الفلكية لها مناهج معينة يمتنع فيها تقدم المتأخر وتأخر المتقدم كانت الحوادث العنصرية كذلك وحينئذ لا حاجة بها إلى المخصص فإن قلت فلم خلق العالم في الوقت المعين، وما خلقه قبل ذلك ولا بعد . قلت هذا إنما يصبح لو كان قبل خلق الفلك وقت وزمان وذلك محال بالاتفاق ، أما عند الفلاسفة فلان الزمان مقدار حركة معدل النهار فقبل وجودها لا يمكن وجود الزمان ، وأما عند المسلمين فلان الزمان محدث ، وإذا كان كذلك فقبل الخلق لا زمان فيستحيل أن يقال لم لم يخلقه فى زمان آخر
We grant possibility, but why is it not permissible to say that God, exalted, created the celestial spheres and created in them moving natures for themselves, and then due to them these events are generated in our world? And if the elemental events are connected to the celestial conjunctions, having determined courses in which it is impossible for the later to precede and the earlier to be delayed, then the elemental events are likewise, and then there is no need for a specificator. Then you say: Then why did He create the world at that specific time, and not create it before or after? I say: This is only valid if there were a time and duration before the creation of the celestial sphere, but that is impossible by agreement. As for the philosophers, time is the measure of the motion of the celestial equator, so before its existence, time cannot exist. As for the Muslims, time is created (muhdath). And if so, before creation there is no time, so it is impossible to say, 'Why did He not create it at another time?'
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection and its answer. The objector, having been granted possibility, argues that events might not need a specificator if they follow from the natures of celestial spheres and their conjunctions. The natural order would determine times. The responder questions why the world was created at a specific moment. The objector's response is that before creation there was no time, so the question is invalid. Razi explains that both philosophers and Muslims agree that time began with the creation of the spheres (for philosophers, time is the measure of motion; for Muslims, time is created). Thus, there was no 'other time' in which the world could have been created. This refutes the objection that God could have created the world earlier or later, thereby preserving the need for a specificator (will) for the specific moment of creation.

Key Terms
أفلاك
طبائع
اتصالات فلكية
زمان
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is an absolute, eternal container that pre-exists creation, so one can ask why God created the world at one moment rather than another.
Correction: Razi shows that time is either a measure of motion (philosophers) or a created entity (Muslims). In either case, there was no time before the creation of the celestial spheres, so the question of 'why this time?' is invalid.

Checkpoint

B) An eternal container of events
C) A created substance
D) The duration of the world

Key Takeaway
Time is created, so there is no prior time to ask why creation happened at that moment; the specific moment of creation is chosen by divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that time itself is a creation of God, and every moment is a direct act of His will. When you think about a painful waiting period in your life, do you truly believe that God chose that exact moment for a specific reason? Or do you secretly think it was random? What would change in your heart if you fully accepted that His timing is as deliberate as the moment of creation?
?
The objector tried to explain away God's specific involvement by appealing to natural causes. In your own life, where have you been attributing events to 'just how things work' or 'coincidence' instead of seeing the specific will of God behind the timing? How can you train yourself to thank Him for the particular moment of every blessing and trial?
?
If there was no 'before' creation, then God's act was uniquely chosen with no prior time to compare. Do you sometimes compare God's timing in your life to an imagined 'better time'? How does the realization that time is a creation help you surrender your own preferences for timing to His perfect will?
