الإجماع ليس إلا على اللفظ أما المعنى الذى يقول أصحابنا فهو غير مجمع علية بل لم يقل به أحد إلا أصحابنا ، والمستمد قوله تعالى ، وكلم الله موسى تكليماء فإن قيل : اسم الكلام موضوع في اللغة لهذه الألفاظ ، وأنتم لا تقولون بكونه تعالى موصوفا بالكلام بهذا المعنى فقد حرفتم اللفظ عن ظاهره ، وإذا كان كذاك لم يكن صرفه إلى المعنى الذي ذكرتموه أولى من فه إلى معنى آخر وهو الأمر الذي عرف الله تعالى ما يفعل بالمكلفين في الآخرة من الثواب والعقاب ، ثم إن نزلنا عنه لكنه إثبات الكلام بالكلام وإثبات الشيء بنفسه باطل . الجواب : أن صرفه إلى هذا المعنى أولى لقول الشاعر : إن الكلام لفى الفؤاد وإنما جعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا . والجواب عن الثاني : إن إثبات كلام الله تعالى بإخبار الرسول والعلم بصدق الرسول لا يتوقف على العلم بكونه متكلما ، لأنا مهما علمنا أنه لا يجوز ظهور المعجزة على الكاذب علمنا صدقه سواء علمنا كلام الله تعالى أو لم تعلمه فهذا منتهى القول في هذه المسألة
Whether the name 'speaker' applies in language to this meaning or not, this is a linguistic discussion with no rational consideration at all, and the speakers of both parties have lengthened it with no benefit. As for our companions, they agreed that Allah is not speaking with speech that is letters and sounds, but rather they claimed that He is a speaker with speech of the soul (kalam al-nafs). The Mu'tazila deny this essence, and if they were to acknowledge it, they would deny that the essence of the Creator is described by it, and if so, they would deny that it is one. So the conclusion is that what they went to we are among those who say it, except we have established another matter and they dispute with us regarding the essence, existence, pre-eternity, and oneness. So this is a preface necessary for those who delve into this issue. The companions argued for His being a speaker with proofs. First: He is living (hayy) and a living being is validly described with speech, so if Allah were not described with it, He would be described with its opposite, which is deficiency, and that is impossible for Allah. The Mu'tazila said: affirmation is preceded by conception, so what is the essence of this speech? For what we find from ourselves is either these letters and sounds or the like of these letters and sounds, and you do not affirm them for Allah. If you say—I mean by 'command' the request for action—I say: why is it not possible that this request is will (irada)? And you, when you attempted to differentiate between it and will, you said that Allah may command what He does not will, but this difference is only established after establishing that He is a speaker, and that depends on conceiving the essence of speech. So if we [attempt to] clarify the essence of speech, circularity would arise. And even if we descend from this position, why did you say that describing Allah's essence with speech is valid? And its explanation is the three aspects mentioned in the issue of hearing and sight. [Second:] Granted that it is valid for Him to be described with it, but why did you say that its opposite is deficiency and defect? Rather, what we consider deficiency and defect in custom is inability to utter letters. As for the opposite of the meaning you mentioned, why did you say that it is deficiency? Rather, if it were said that this meaning itself is deficiency, it would be closer, because establishing command and prohibition in the absence of an addressee is foolishness (safah) and that is deficiency. And the remaining questions have preceded. Second: Since we know that Allah's actions allow advancement and delay, we refer them to a preponderator, which is will. Similarly, we see the actions of servants fluctuating between prohibition, permission, recommendation, and obligation, so their specification by these rulings requires a particularizer, and that is not will, because Allah may command what He does not will, and conversely. So there must be another attribute, which is speech. This is also weak, for we say: why is it not permissible that the meaning of obligation and prohibition is that Allah made known to the responsible person that He wills to punish the one who leaves such an act in the afterlife, or wills to grant reward to him in the afterlife? This extent has no need for establishing speech. If you claim something beyond that, it is denied. Third: Allah is a sovereign obeyed, and the obeyed is one who has command and prohibition. This is very weak, because if they mean by 'obeyed' the penetration of His power and will in creatures, it is granted; if they mean that He has command and prohibition, that is the very point of the issue. Fourth: The consensus of Muslims on His being a speaker. This is weak because we have shown that consensus is only about the word; as for the meaning that our companions hold, it is not agreed upon, rather no one holds it except our companions. And the proof is His saying: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly.' If it is said: the word 'speech' is placed in language for these utterances, and you do not affirm that Allah is described with speech in this sense, so you have distorted the word from its apparent meaning. If so, turning it to the meaning you mentioned is not more appropriate than turning it to another meaning, which is the command by which Allah made known what He will do to the responsible persons in the afterlife of reward and punishment. Then even if we descend from this, it is establishing speech by speech, and establishing a thing by itself is invalid. The answer: that turning it to this meaning is more appropriate due to the poet's saying: 'Speech is in the heart, and the tongue is made a proof upon the heart.' And the answer to the second: that establishing Allah's speech by the report of the messenger and knowledge of the truthfulness of the messenger does not depend on knowing that He is a speaker, because once we know that it is not permissible for a miracle to appear upon a liar, we know his truthfulness whether we know Allah's speech or not. This is the end of the discussion on this issue.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and refutation concerning the attribute of speech (kalam). Razi begins by noting that the dispute over the term 'speaker' (mutakallim) is merely linguistic and has no rational benefit. He then states the Ash'ari position: Allah is a speaker with a speech that is a meaning in the soul (kalam nafsi), not letters or sounds. The Mu'tazila deny this essence entirely. Razi presents four proofs from the Ash'ari school and then answers the objections. The first proof: Allah is living (hayy), and a living being can be described with speech; if He were not described with speech, He would be described with its opposite (silence or inability), which is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah. The Mu'tazila object that we do not even understand the essence of speech as a meaning in the soul; what we find is only utterances. They also question whether the opposite is truly a deficiency; rather, the inability to utter letters is deficiency, not the lack of an inner meaning. The second proof: since the actions of servants are specified with rulings like obligation and prohibition, these require a particularizer (mukhassis), and that particularizer cannot be will (irada) because God sometimes commands what He does not will; so there must be another attribute, which is speech. The Mu'tazila respond by reducing obligation and prohibition to expressions of divine will about punishment and reward, which does not require a separate attribute. The third proof: Allah is a king obeyed (malik muta'), and the obeyed is the one who commands and prohibits. Razi dismisses this as weak because if 'obeyed' means His power, it is irrelevant; if it means command and prohibition, it begs the question. The fourth proof: consensus (ijma') that Allah is a speaker. Razi notes that consensus is only on the word, not the inner meaning held by Ash'aris. The opponent then objects that the Quranic verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' refers to audible speech, and the Ash'aris distort its apparent meaning. Razi answers with the famous poetic line that 'speech is in the heart' to show that the term can refer to inner speech. The second objection is that using the verse to prove speech is circular; Razi answers that the proof of prophecy through miracles does not depend on prior knowledge of divine speech. The lesson ends by concluding the issue.

Key Terms
الفؤاد
اللسان
دليل
المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari interpretation of the verse contradicts the Arabic language.
Correction: The poet's line demonstrates that 'kalam' is used for inner meaning, so the interpretation is linguistically valid.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the charge that using the Quran to prove divine speech is circular?
A) He says the Quran is not speech.
B) He argues that we first know God is a speaker through reason.
C) He says we know the Prophet is truthful through miracles, then accept the Quran, which teaches divine speech.
D) He says the circularity is unavoidable.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends the Ash'ari position by showing that 'kalam' can refer to inner speech and that the proof of prophecy avoids circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi uses a line of poetry to show that 'kalam' can be inner speech. When you speak to Allah in du'a or dhikr, do you think He hears your outer voice or your inner thoughts? Which one reflects your true sincerity?
?
The tongue is called a 'proof upon the heart.' Think about the last time your words did not match your inner state. Are you comfortable with the gap? What would it take for your heart and tongue to align perfectly before Allah?
?
Razi avoids circularity by grounding proof of prophecy in miracles. Do you trust in Allah's message because of external signs, or because of something deeper? If all miracles vanished, would your certainty remain? What would that mean for your faith today?

The Attribute of Permanence: A Refutation of an Extraneous Meaning
Razi refutes the Ash'ari view that Allah is permanent by a meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being does not require a separate attribute of permanence and the logical problems that follow from positing such an attribute.

Core Idea
Permanence as a separate attribute is only meaningful for contingent beings, not for the Necessary Being.

In this passage, Razi engages with the Ash'ari view that Allah's permanence is due to an attribute called baqa. He argues that this concept is rationally unnecessary for the Necessary Being and leads to logical absurdities such as infinite regress or circularity. We will examine his arguments and the objection he considers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
Read the first line to see the positions of al-Ash'ari and his followers versus al-Qadi and al-Juwayni. Razi declares the latter as the truth.
This sets up the debate.
2
Understand the definition of baqa
Razi defines baqa as an attribute that preponderates existence over non-existence. Notice that this definition is only applicable to possible beings.
3
Follow the argument from necessity
Razi argues that the Necessary Being does not need an external cause for its continued existence. Baqa as a preponderator is superfluous for the Necessary Being.
4
Analyze the regress and circularity arguments
If baqa itself is permanent, it would require another baqa, leading to infinite regress or circularity. Also, if baqa is permanent by itself while the essence depends on it, the roles reverse, which is impossible.
5
Consider the objection from the witnessed world
Razi presents an objection: in the physical world, permanence is just the substance's existence in the second moment, not an additional entity. This undermines the need for a separate attribute.
The text ends here, but the objection works in favor of Razi's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: ذهب أبو الحسن الأشعرى وأتباعه إلى أن الله تعالى باق ببقاء يقوم به وذهب القاضى وإمام الحرمين إلى نفيه وهو الحق لنا المعقول من اللبقاء صفة تقتضى ترجيح الوجود على الأعدم هذا إنما يعقل فى حق ممكن الوجود، فواجب الوجود لذاته يستحيل أن يكون رجحان وجوده على عدمه معللا بمعنى، وأيضا فذلك البقاء لا شك أنه باق فإن كان باقيا ببقاء آخر لزم إما التسلسل وإما الدور ، إن كان باقيا ببقاء الذات التى فرضناها باقية بذلك البقاء ، وإن كان باقيا بنفسه ويكون الذات باقية مفتقرة إليه انقلب الذات صفة والصفة ذاتا وهو محال، وأماني الشاهد فليس بمعنى أيضا لأن شرط حصوله في الجوهر حصول الجوهر فى الزمان الثانى ، فلو افتقر حصول الجوهر في الزمان الثاني إليه لزم الدور . ولقائل أن يقول : البقاء نفس حصول الجوهر فى الزمان الثانى ، لا أنه أمر موقوف عليه
Issue: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his followers held that Allah is permanent (baqi) by a permanence (baqa) subsisting in Him. Al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn held its negation, and it is the truth. For us, the rational understanding of permanence is an attribute that necessitates the preponderance of existence over non-existence. This is only conceivable for a possible being (mumkin al-wujud). As for the Necessary Being in essence (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi), it is impossible that the preponderance of its existence over non-existence be caused by a meaning. Also, that permanence is undoubtedly permanent. So if it is permanent by another permanence, either infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr) would follow, if it is permanent by the permanence of the essence which we assumed is permanent by that permanence. And if it is permanent by itself, while the essence is permanent and dependent upon it, then the essence becomes an attribute and the attribute becomes an essence, which is impossible. As for analogical evidence from the witnessed world (shahid), it is not a meaning either, because the condition for its occurrence in a substance is the occurrence of the substance in the second moment. So if the occurrence of the substance in the second moment were dependent upon it, circularity would follow. And one may say: permanence is the very occurrence of the substance in the second moment, not something dependent upon it.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion (mas'ala) on the attribute of permanence (baqa). Razi presents the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his followers that Allah is permanent by a meaning (ma'na) called baqa that subsists in Him. Against this, Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) held that there is no such attribute, and Razi declares this to be the truth (al-haqq). He then provides the evidence (lana) from rational consideration (al-ma'qul): permanence, rationally, is an attribute that gives preponderance to existence over non-existence. But such a preponderator is only needed for a contingent possible being, not for the Necessary Being whose existence is essential. Thus, attributing baqa as a separate meaning is unnecessary. Next, he argues that if baqa itself is permanent, it would require another baqa, leading to infinite regress or circularity. If baqa is permanent by the same essence's baqa, that is circular; if baqa is permanent by itself while the essence depends on it, then the roles of attribute and essence would be reversed, which is impossible. He then addresses analogical reasoning (tamthil) from the witnessed world: in substances, permanence is conditional upon the substance's existence in a second moment, so if that existence itself depended on permanence, circularity would recur. Finally, an objector says that permanence is simply the substance's existence in the second moment, not an additional entity. The text ends without a clear response from Razi, but the objection is presented as a counterpoint; the implication is that this view undermines the need for a separate attribute. The commentary notes that Razi supports the negation of baqa as a separate attribute.

Key Terms
البقاء
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود
التسلسل
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Ash'aris affirm that Allah has an attribute of permanence (baqa) as a meaning subsisting in Him.
Correction: Notable Ash'ari scholars like Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) denied this, and Razi sides with them. The view is debated within the school.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say baqa as an attribute is only conceivable for possible beings?
A) Because only possible beings have a beginning.
B) Because baqa is defined as giving preponderance to existence over non-existence, which only possible beings need.
C) Because possible beings are temporal.
D) Because baqa is an accident, which cannot subsist in the Necessary Being.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the Necessary Being does not require a separate attribute of permanence, as positing one leads to logical fallacies such as infinite regress or circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ponder Allah's attribute of permanence (baqa), do you unconsciously imagine Him as just another being who simply never stops existing, like an unending creature? How does realizing that Allah's existence is necessary by itself (wajib li-dhatihi) and not sustained by any cause change the way you depend on Him? What practical difference does it make in your trust in His continuity?
?
Razi argues that positing a separate attribute of permanence for Allah leads to logical contradictions like infinite regress. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah's attributes that, upon honest reflection, might lead to similar logical problems? What would it cost you to correct them?
?
If Allah's attribute of permanence is not a separate meaning but simply His essential existence, how does that affect your worship? Is there a part of your ibadah that treats Allah as if He needs something to sustain His existence, even subconsciously? How can you align your heart with the reality that He is al-Baqi by Himself alone?

Refuting the 'Obeyed King' Proof
Razi refutes the argument that God is an obeyed king.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses a logical disjunction to refute the argument that God's being an obeyed king proves He has speech.

Core Idea
Being obeyed does not prove speech—either it's about power or it's the very point in dispute.

Imam al-Razi presents a third proof for God's speech and then dismantles it with a simple logical disjunction. This lesson shows how a seemingly plausible analogy can be exposed as either irrelevant or circular. You will see the precision of kalam critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the structure: a short argument followed by a refutation.
2
Identify the disjunction
Razi examines two possible meanings of 'obeyed'. Find where he says 'if they mean... then...' and 'if they mean... then...'. Understand each branch.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Reflect on the refutation's strength
Ask yourself why this argument is called 'very weak'. How does showing that a premise is either conceded or circular undermine the proof?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : أن الله تعالى ملك مطاع والمطاع هو الذى له الأمر والنهي وهو ضعيف جدا ، لأنهم إن عنوا بالمطاع نفوذ قدرته ومشيئته في المخلوقات فهو مسلم وإن عنوا به أن له أمرا ونهيا فهو أول المسألة .
And the third [proof]: that God, exalted is He, is a king obeyed, and the obeyed is the one who has command and prohibition, and it is very weak, because if they mean by 'obeyed' the penetration of His power and will in created beings, then it is conceded; but if they mean by it that He has command and prohibition, then it is the beginning of the issue.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a third proof that God is a speaker (mutakallim) based on Him being an obeyed king. Razi states the argument: God is a king who is obeyed, and the obeyed one is the one who issues commands and prohibitions. He then refutes it by a disjunction: if by 'obeyed' they mean the effective power of His will over creation, then the premise is accepted, but it doesn't prove speech; if they mean He has command and prohibition, that is exactly what is being debated (the beginning of the issue), so the argument is circular. Thus the proof is weak.

Key Terms
مطاع (obeyed)
أمر ونهي (command and prohibition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being obeyed necessarily implies speech, because in human affairs commanders are obeyed.
Correction: Razi shows that being obeyed can refer to effective power, which does not require verbal command, or it simply restates the claim, making the argument circular.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's response to the argument that God is an obeyed king?
A) He accepts it as proof of God's power.
B) He says it is either a conceded fact about God's power or it merely restates the issue.
C) He says it is a strong proof.
D) He says the argument is invalid because God does not command.

Key Takeaway
A proof that merely restates the claim or relies on an irrelevant meaning does not establish the desired attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God as 'obeyed,' do you focus on His power making things happen, or on His commands to you? Has this lesson revealed that you might be confusing the two? How would your relationship with God change if you saw His obedience more as His will being done than as a spoken command?
?
In your own reasoning about faith, have you ever assumed a conclusion because you used a term in two different ways without noticing? Take a moment to identify a belief you hold about God that might rest on such an equivocation. What would it take to check it against the source?
?
Razi's point is that a weak proof can be spotted if it either concedes something irrelevant or assumes the very thing to be proven. Think of a time you accepted an argument about God's attributes without examining it closely. What did you learn about intellectual honesty from this lesson?

Razi's Refutation of the Fourth Proof: Ijma and the Verse of Musa
Razi refutes the claim that ijma and the verse of Musa prove God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of using consensus and the verse of Musa to prove that God's speech is an eternal attribute, and his two responses to objections based on linguistic and logical grounds.

Core Idea
Consensus only confirms the word 'kalam,' not its theological meaning; the verse of Musa is not circular evidence.

Razi now tackles the fourth proof advanced by those who claim God's speech is an eternal, incorporeal attribute. This proof appeals to the consensus of Muslims and to the verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses with speech.' Razi shows that consensus does not extend to the specific Ash'ari interpretation, and he addresses two objections to his interpretation of the verse. This passage is a classic example of intra-Islamic theological debate, blending linguistic and logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Razi first dismisses the consensus argument briefly, then focuses on the verse.
2
Identify the two parts of the refutation
The passage has a clear structure: refutation of ijma, then an objection regarding the verse, followed by two answers. Identify each part as you read.
3
Understand the objection and Razi's first answer
The objector claims that the Ash'ari interpretation of 'kalam' as inner speech is arbitrary. Razi counters with a line of poetry that shows inner speech is the primary meaning. Reflect on why this poetic evidence is persuasive.
The poetry is attributed to a pre-Islamic or early Islamic poet; it shows the linguistic usage.
4
Examine Razi's second answer
The second objection is that using the verse as proof is circular (proving speech by speech). Razi argues that the messenger's truthfulness is established by miracles, independent of knowing God's speech. Consider how this avoids circularity.
This answer relies on the principle that miracles confirm prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : إجماع المسلمين على كونه متكلما وهو ضعيف لما بينا أن الإجماع ليس إلا على اللفظ أما المعنى الذى يقول أصحابنا فهو غير مجمع علية بل لم يقل به أحد إلا أصحابنا ، والمستمد قوله تعالى ، وكلم الله موسى تكليماء فإن قيل : اسم الكلام موضوع في اللغة لهذه الألفاظ ، وأنتم لا تقولون بكونه تعالى موصوفا بالكلام بهذا المعنى فقد حرفتم اللفظ عن ظاهره ، وإذا كان كذاك لم يكن صرفه إلى المعنى الذي ذكرتموه أولى من فه إلى معنى آخر وهو الأمر الذي عرف الله تعالى ما يفعل بالمكلفين في الآخرة من الثواب والعقاب ، ثم إن نزلنا عنه لكنه إثبات الكلام بالكلام وإثبات الشيء بنفسه باطل صرفه الجواب : أن صرفه إلى هذا المعنى أولى لقول الشاعر : إن الكلام لفى الفؤاد وإنما جعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا والجواب عن الثاني : إن إثبات كلام الله تعالى بإخبار الرسول والعلم بصدق الرسول لا يتوقف على العلم بكونه متكلما ، لأنا مهما علمنا أنه لا يجوز ظهور المعجزة على الكاذب علمنا صدقه سواء علمنا كلام الله تعالى أو لم تعلمه فهذا منتهى القول في هذه المسألة
And the fourth [proof]: the consensus of the Muslims on His being a speaker, and it is weak because we have explained that consensus is only on the expression; as for the meaning which our companions hold, it is not agreed upon, rather no one except our companions has stated it. And the source is His statement, 'And Allah spoke to Moses with [direct] speech.' If it is said: the word 'speech' (kalam) is placed in language for these utterances, and you do not say that God is described with speech in this meaning, so you have twisted the expression from its apparent meaning, and if so, then diverting it to the meaning you mentioned is not more appropriate than diverting it to another meaning — namely, the matter which God made known to the obligated beings regarding reward and punishment in the afterlife. Then, if we concede that, it is proving speech by speech, and proving a thing by itself is invalid. The answer: that diverting it to this meaning is more appropriate due to the poet's saying: 'Indeed speech lies in the heart, and the tongue is made only a guide to the heart.' And the answer to the second [objection]: that proving the speech of God through the report of the messenger and the knowledge of the truthfulness of the messenger does not depend on the knowledge of His being a speaker, because whenever we know that it is not permissible for a miracle to appear on a liar, we know his truthfulness whether we know the speech of God or not. So this is the end of the discussion on this issue.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage contains Razi's refutation of the fourth proof for God's speech, which is consensus (ijma) and the verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses with speech'. He first weakens the consensus argument, stating that consensus only exists on the word 'kalam', not on the particular meaning the Ash'aris give it (that it is an eternal attribute). Then he addresses the verse. An objector says: the word 'kalam' literally means these audible utterances, and you (Ash'aris) do not ascribe such speech to God, so you are interpreting metaphorically. If you can interpret it metaphorically, others can interpret it differently (e.g., as intending reward and punishment). Moreover, if you use the verse as proof, you are proving speech by speech (since the verse itself is speech), which is circular. Razi gives two answers: first, the metaphorical interpretation of 'kalam' as meaning what is in the heart (inner speech) is more appropriate, supported by a line of poetry that distinguishes between internal speech and its expression. Second, the proof of God's speech through the messenger's report does not require prior knowledge that God is a speaker; the proof of the messenger's truthfulness (via miracles) suffices. Thus the alleged circularity is avoided.

Key Terms
إجماع (consensus)
لفظ (expression)
ظاهر (apparent meaning)
كلام (speech)
قلب (heart)
معجزة (miracle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ijma proves that God's speech is an eternal attribute in the Ash'ari sense.
Correction: Razi clarifies that ijma only covers the word 'kalam,' not the specific theological interpretation of it as an eternal, incorporeal attribute.

Checkpoint
How does Razi counter the objection that interpreting 'kalam' in the verse metaphorically is arbitrary?
A) He says no interpretation is needed.
B) He cites a poet to show that inner speech is the proper meaning.
C) He says the verse is not about God's speech.
D) He says the objector's interpretation leads to heresy.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the proof from consensus and the verse of Musa by limiting consensus to the word, not the meaning, and by providing a poetic justification for metaphorical interpretation and a non-circular argument for God's speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse of the Quran that seems to contradict what you believe, do you immediately assume your belief is wrong, or do you seek a deeper interpretation as Razi does with the verse of Musa? Which approach truly reflects trust in Allah's wisdom and the scholars He raised?
?
Razi uses a line of poetry to support his interpretation. How often do you rely on evidence from sources beyond the Quran and Sunnah to strengthen your faith? Are you willing to study the tools of interpretation (like Arabic poetry, grammar, and logic) to avoid misunderstanding Allah's words?
?
The objector accuses Razi of twisting the apparent meaning. Have you ever been accused of 'twisting' religious texts when you tried to reconcile them with your understanding? How did you respond — with arrogance or humility? Will you commit today to learning the principles of sound interpretation so that your defense of the truth is based on knowledge, not stubbornness?

God's Permanence: Is It an Added Attribute?
Razi states positions and argues against baqa' as an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that God's permanence is not an attribute added to His essence, and why it is a distinctive property of the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
For the Necessary Existent, permanence is not an extra attribute—it is essential.

Razi opens the discussion on the attribute of permanence (baqa') by presenting two opposing views. He then advances a powerful argument that exposes a fundamental flaw in the idea that God's permanence could be a separate attribute. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the first sentence of the passage to distinguish the view of al-Ash'ari and his followers from that of al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn. Note which view Razi endorses.
Razi calls the latter 'the truth'.
2
Understand the definition of baqa'
Examine Razi's definition: baqa' is an attribute that necessitates the preference of existence over non-existence. See why this definition only applies to contingent beings.
For the Necessary Existent, existence is not caused by any additional meaning.
3
Follow the regress and circularity argument
Razi argues that if baqa' were an attribute, it itself would need to be permanent, leading to infinite regress, circularity, or inversion of essence and attribute. Work through each possibility.
This shows the logical impossibility of a superadded attribute of permanence.
4
Consider the argument from the witnessed world
Razi adds that even in the empirical world, permanence cannot be a separate attribute because of circularity.
This reinforces the argument from contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: ذهب أبو الحسن الأشعرى وأتباعه إلى أن الله تعالى باق ببقاء يقوم به (*) وذهب القاضى وإمام الحرمين إلى نفيه وهو الحق ) لنا المعقول من اللبقاء صفة تقتضى ترجيح الوجود على الأعدم هذا إنما يعقل فى حق ممكن الوجود، فواجب الوجود لذاته يستحيل أن يكون رجحان وجوده على عدمه معللا بمعنى، وأيضا فذلك البقاء لا شك أنه باق فإن كان باقيا ببقاء آخر لزم إما التسلسل وإما الدور ، إن كان باقيا ببقاء الذات التى فرضناها باقية بذلك البقاء ، وإن كان باقيا بنفسه ويكون الذات باقية مفتقرة إليه انقلب الذات صفة والصفة ذاتا وهو محال، وأماني الشاهد فليس بمعنى أيضا لأن شرط حصوله في الجوهر حصول الجوهر فى الزمان الثانى ، فلو افتقر حصول الجوهر في الزمان الثاني إليه لزم الدور .
Issue: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his followers held that God is permanent (baqi) by a permanence (baqa') subsisting in Him. And al-Qadi [al-Baqillani] and Imam al-Haramayn [al-Juwayni] held its negation, and it is the truth. Our proof: The rationally understood from permanence is an attribute that necessitates the preference of existence over non-existence; this is only conceivable in the case of a possible existent. As for the Necessary Existent by essence, it is impossible that the preference of its existence over its non-existence be caused by a meaning. Also, that permanence is undoubtedly permanent. If it is permanent by another permanence, either infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr) follows. If it is permanent by the permanence of the essence which we supposed to be permanent by that permanence [i.e., the attribute], [this leads to circularity]. And if it is permanent by itself, and the essence is permanent in need of it, then the essence becomes an attribute and the attribute becomes an essence, which is impossible. And as for that in the witnessed [world], it is not a meaning either, because the condition of its occurrence in a substance is the occurrence of the substance in the second time; so if the occurrence of the substance in the second time were in need of it, circularity would follow.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the discussion on the attribute of baqa' (permanence). Razi first states the two positions: Ash'ari holds that God is permanent by a superadded attribute of baqa', while al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) deny that, and Razi endorses the latter as correct. He then presents an argument: the concept of permanence as an attribute that tips the scale from nonexistence to existence is only meaningful for contingent beings; for the Necessary Existent, its existence is not caused by any meaning. Moreover, if permanence is an attribute, it itself must be permanent, leading to either infinite regress if it requires another permanence, or circularity if it is permanent by the essence that it is supposed to make permanent, or an inversion of essence and attribute if it is self-permanent while the essence depends on it. Finally, he argues even in the empirical world, permanence is not a separate attribute because its occurrence depends on the substance's existence in the second time, so if the substance's existence depended on it, it would be circular.

Key Terms
باق (permanent)
بقاء (permanence)
ممكن (contingent)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since God is permanent, He must have an attribute of permanence.
Correction: Razi shows that for the Necessary Existent, permanence is not an additional attribute; it is identical with His essence, and positing a separate attribute leads to logical contradictions.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that baqa' cannot be an attribute of God?
A) Because God is not permanent.
B) Because the concept of baqa' implies causation which is inappropriate for the Necessary Existent.
C) Because the Quran does not mention it.
D) Because al-Ash'ari did not hold that view.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that applying the concept of permanence as a superadded attribute to God leads to logical contradictions, thus affirming that God's permanence is identical with His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking of God as needing something—even an attribute—to sustain His existence? What does that assumption reveal about how you view His perfection?
?
If God's essence is identical with His permanence, does your trust in His eternal nature feel more secure, or does it unsettle you that He does not fit into the categories you use for creation?
?
What changes in your daily worship when you internalize that God's existence is not caused or sustained by anything—not even by an attribute of permanence? How can you remind yourself of this tomorrow?

Two Objections to God's Permanence as an Attribute
Razi presents two objections and responses on divine permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the attribute of permanence (baqa') by responding to objections that try to reduce it to mere temporal existence or infer it from change.

Core Idea
Permanence is not just existence in a second moment; it is a real attribute.

This passage presents two objections against the view that divine permanence is an additional attribute. The first objection reduces permanence to mere occurrence in a second time; the second argues that change from non-permanent to permanent implies an extra attribute. Razi counters each with logical arguments, showing the opponents' reasoning leads to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of objection and answer. Notice the structure: objector says X, Razi says Y.
This passage has two clear objection-answer pairs and a final exchange.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the first objection: 'Permanence is nothing but the occurrence of the substance in the second time.' Then find the second: 'The essence was not permanent at the time of coming-to-be, then it becomes permanent, so permanence must be additional.'
3
Follow Razi's responses
For the first objection, Razi says mere occurrence in time is not an attribute (otherwise infinite regress). For the second, he uses a parity argument: by the same logic, coming-to-be (hadath) would be an attribute, which is false.
Notice the symmetry in the final exchange: if hadath is first-time existence, then baqa' is second-time existence.
4
Reflect on the strategy
Razi uses reductio ad absurdum and parity arguments. He does not accept the premise that change necessarily implies an additional attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : البقاء نفس حصول الجوهر فى الزمان الثانى ، لا أنه أمر موقوف عليه . وجوابه أن نفس الحصول في الزمان ليس صفة وإلا لزم التسلسل احتجوا بأن الذات لم تكن باقية حال الحدوث ثم صارت باقية فوجب أن يكون البقاء زائداً . والجواب : بأنه معارض بأن الذات كانت حادثة زمان الحدوث ثم حال البقاء ما بقيت حادثة فيلزم أن يكون الحدوث صفة زائدة وهو محال على ما تقدم . فإن قلت : الحدوث نفس حصوله في الزمان الأول. قلت : البقاء نفس حصوله في الزمان الثانى
And an objector may say: 'Permanence is nothing but the occurrence of the substance in the second time, not something upon which it depends.' Its answer: That mere occurrence in time is not an attribute, otherwise infinite regress would follow. They also argued that the essence was not permanent at the time of its coming-to-be, then it becomes permanent, so permanence must be additional. The answer: It is countered by the fact that the essence was coming-to-be at the time of its origination, then at the time of permanence it does not remain coming-to-be, so it would follow that coming-to-be (hadath) is an additional attribute, and that is impossible as previously shown. If you say: 'Coming-to-be is just its occurrence in the first time', I say: 'Permanence is just its occurrence in the second time.'
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage contains two rounds of objection and answer. First, someone objects that permanence is simply the substance's existence in the second moment of time, not a separate attribute. Razi responds that mere occurrence in time is not a real attribute, because then there would be infinite regress of such occurrences. Second, the opponents argue that since the essence changes from non-permanent to permanent, there must be an additional attribute of permanence. Razi counters by parallel: the essence also changes from being 'coming-to-be' (hadith) to not being so, so if that requires an attribute, then hadath would also be an attribute, which is unacceptable (since hadath is a relation). The final exchange clarifies that if one defines hadath as mere first-time existence, then baqa' is mere second-time existence, and neither is a real attribute.

Key Terms
جوهر (substance)
زمان (time)
حدوث (coming-to-be/origination)
صفة (attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A change in state (e.g., from non-permanent to permanent) necessarily implies an additional attribute.
Correction: Razi shows that the same logic would imply that coming-to-be (hadath) is an attribute, which is false. Hence the argument is invalid.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to the objection that the essence changes from non-permanent to permanent, thus requiring an attribute?
A) He denies that the essence changes.
B) He says the same argument would prove that coming-to-be (hadath) is an attribute, which is false.
C) He accepts the argument.
D) He says permanence is not an attribute but a relation.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends the attribute of permanence by showing that attempts to reduce it to temporal existence or infer it from change lead to absurdities or unwanted parallels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows the danger of reducing divine attributes to mere relational concepts. Is there an attribute of God in your heart that you have mentally reduced to just 'the way He relates to me' (like mercy, anger, etc.)? Will you confess that as a real attribute of His essence and worship Him accordingly today?
?
The opponents thought that change from non-permanent to permanent required an additional attribute, but Razi showed that not every change implies a real attribute. When your faith changes or deepens, do you assume that change is just your own psychological shift, or could it reflect something real about God's interaction with you? How does this lesson affect your view of spiritual growth?
?
If you were asked, 'Is God's permanence just Him continuing to exist?' would you answer like Razi's objectors or like Razi? What is at stake for your reverence of God if you deny that permanence is a real attribute?

The Third Position on Baqa': Rejecting Divine Permanence
Commentator presents and critiques al-Ka'bi's view on baqa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why applying the attribute of baqa' (permanence) to Allah is considered a form of anthropomorphism according to the commentator.

Core Idea
Baqa' implies temporal duration; applying it to God is anthropomorphism.

In this passage, the commentator critiques a third position on baqa' held by al-Ka'bi, who affirms permanence for contingent beings but denies it for God. The commentator argues that defining baqa' as an attribute that tips the scale of existence over non-existence is not unique, and the real meaning of baqa' involves existence across multiple times. This leads to the conclusion that applying baqa' to the timeless God is a form of anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense argumentation style.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's position
Find where the commentator states the third position: baqa' is affirmed for contingents but denied for God. Note that al-Ka'bi held this view.
3
Understand the argument against baqa' as an attribute
The commentator examines the claim that baqa' is an attribute that prefers existence. He counters that even non-permanent things have such preference unless it refers to the second time. This shows baqa' is not unique.
4
Follow the verification and analogy
The commentator verifies that baqa' means existence for more than one time, which is inconceivable for a non-temporal being. He uses the analogy of the 'whole greater than part' judgment, which is not temporal.
This strengthens the argument that temporal concepts cannot apply to God.
5
Conclude with the anthropomorphism charge
The commentator concludes that describing God with baqa' is a form of tashbih (anthropomorphism) with temporal things. Reflect on why this is a serious charge in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : وههذا مذهب آخر وهو يقول بثبوت البقاء في الممكنات ونفيه عنه تعالى وبه قال الكعبي وأتباعه . قوله البقاء صفة تقتضى ترجيح الوجود على العدم فقال له الموجود الذى لا يبقى له بدله أيضاً ما يقتضى ترجح وجوده على عدمه فإذا هذا الحكم ليس ما يختص بالبقاء ، إلا أن يكون الترجيح إلى الزمان الثاني ، والتحقيق فيه أن البقاء مقارنة الوجود لاكثر من زمان واحد بعد الزمان الأول وذلك لا يعقل فما لا يكون زمانيا ، واعتبر الحكم بكون الكل أعظم من جزئه فإنه لا يمكن أن يقال إنه واقع في زمان أو في جميع الأزمنة كما لا يقال إنه واقع في مكان أو في جميع الأمكنة، وإذا كان الحكم كذلك فما يتوقف عليه الحكم كالتصورات أولى بأن يكون كذلك ، وعلة الزمان لا يكون زمانيا فكيف مبدأ الكل ، فإذا اتصافه بالبقاء نوع من التشبيه بالزمانيات ، وأما كون البقاء باقيا أو غير باق وإن كان باقيا فبقاؤه إما بذاته أو بغيره فحكمه حكم الأمور الاعتبارية التي توجد في العقل فقط وتنقطع عند عدم الاعتبار . وقوله : وأما في الشاهد فليس بمعنى أيضاً إشارة إلى إبطال مذهب الكمى . وقوله : يلى الحدوث ليس صفة زائدة لجوابه ما مر ثم إن كان الحدوث، نفس الحصول في الزمان الأول فالبقاء حصول في زمان مشروط بحصول فى زمان قبله وإلا لم يمكن زمانه ثابتا ، والحصول في الزمان الأول ليس مشروطا بالحصول فى زمان آخر فالاختلاف بينهما بوجود هذا الشرط و من هذا الاعتبار يتحقق ما قلنا وهو أن البقاء مقارنة الوجود لأكثر من زمان واحد بعد الزمان الأول . وعدمه فقط
(1) I say: And this is another position: it affirms permanence in contingents and denies it of Him, exalted, and al-Ka'bi and his followers held this. His statement: 'permanence is an attribute that necessitates the preference of existence over non-existence' – so one says: the existent that does not have permanence also has what necessitates the preponderance of its existence over non-existence, so this judgment is not specific to permanence, unless the preponderance is to the second time. The verification of it is that permanence is the conjunction of existence for more than one time after the first time, and that is not conceivable for what is not temporal. Consider the judgment that the whole is greater than its part: it cannot be said that it occurs in time or in all times, just as it cannot be said that it occurs in place or in all places. And when the judgment is like that, then what the judgment depends on, like concepts, is even more so. And the cause of time is not temporal, so how about the Principle of all? So His being described with permanence is a kind of anthropomorphism (tashbih) with temporal things. As for whether permanence is permanent or not, and if it is permanent, its permanence is either by itself or by another; its ruling is the ruling of mental considerations that exist only in the mind and cease when the consideration ceases. And his statement: 'As for in the witnessed world, it is not a meaning either' is an indication of refuting the position of al-Ka'bi. And his statement: 'following hadath, it is not an additional attribute' – I refer to the previous answer. Then, if hadath is just occurrence in the first time, then permanence is occurrence in a time conditioned by occurrence in a time before it, otherwise its time would not be established. And occurrence in the first time is not conditioned by occurrence in another time; the difference between them is by the existence of this condition. And from this consideration, what we said is verified: that permanence is the conjunction of existence for more than one time after the first time. And its non-existence only.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This is a lengthy marginal commentary (by a later scholar) analyzing the baqa' debate. The commentator first notes a third position: that baqa' is affirmed for contingents but denied for God, held by al-Ka'bi. Then he examines the argument that baqa' is an attribute that tips the scale of existence over non-existence, and notes that even non-permanent things have some ground for existence, so it's not unique to baqa' unless it refers to duration. He then argues that baqa' essentially means existence for more than one time, which is meaningless for a timeless being. He draws an analogy with the judgment that the whole is greater than the part, which is not temporal. Applying temporal concepts to God is a form of anthropomorphism. He then discusses whether baqa' itself would need to be permanent, leading to a regress, and says it is a mental consideration only. Finally, he connects the earlier statement about hadath to conclude that baqa' is merely existence after a previous time, not an attribute.

Key Terms
ممكنات (contingents)
تشبيه (anthropomorphism)
علة الزمان (cause of time)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqa' can be applied to God as an attribute without implying temporality.
Correction: Baqa' means existence for more than one time, which cannot apply to the timeless God; doing so is anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does baqa' essentially mean?
A) An attribute that prefers existence over non-existence
B) Existence for more than one time after the first
C) The opposite of hadath
D) A quality of eternal beings

Key Takeaway
Applying baqa' to God is ruled out because it implies temporal duration, constituting anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often speak of God's attributes in temporal terms—'His mercy endures,' 'His patience lasts.' After this lesson, are you willing to let go of those comforting but limiting images, even if it feels less intimate? What specific phrase can you replace with a more transcendent description?
?
The commentator says that describing God with baqa' is tashbih, a form of likening Him to creation. Reflect: Is there any attribute you habitually think of in a way that subtly confines God to your own experience of time and change? How can you actively purify your conception of God starting today?
?
If God is beyond time, then your entire relationship with Him—prayer, repentance, hope—cannot be measured by duration. How does that challenge the way you evaluate your spiritual progress? What would change if you stopped counting days and instead focused on the quality of your devotion right now?

Baqa' as a Mental Consideration and Its Implications
Commentator analyzes baqa' as a mental consideration and defines it relative to hadath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator reduces baqa' to a mental consideration and distinguishes it from hadath.

Core Idea
Baqa' is not a real attribute but a mental consideration; it is merely existence after a prior time.

In this second part of the commentary, the author takes the argument further, showing that even the concept of permanence (baqa') falls into logical problems if treated as a real attribute. He argues that baqa' itself would require either self-subsistence or dependence, both problematic, and should be regarded as a mental concept that ceases with the act of consideration. He then connects this to the previous discussion on hadath, concluding that baqa' is simply existence conditioned by a prior time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the logical dilemma about whether baqa' is permanent or not.
2
Understand the regress argument
The commentator asks: If baqa' is permanent, its permanence is either by itself or by another, leading to regress or dependence. If not permanent, it is transient. Note how this undermines baqa' as a real attribute.
3
Grasp the concept of mental considerations
The commentator concludes that baqa' is like mental considerations that exist only in the mind and cease when considered. This is a key move in Islamic philosophical theology.
4
Connect to hadath (coming-to-be)
The commentator refers to an earlier statement: 'following hadath, it is not an additional attribute.' He explains that if hadath is occurrence in the first time, then baqa' is occurrence in a time conditioned by a prior time. This shows baqa' is not an independent attribute but a relation.
5
Synthesize the final definition
The commentator reiterates that baqa' is simply the conjunction of existence for more than one time after the first. Reflect on how this definition avoids attributing real eternity to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : وههذا مذهب آخر وهو يقول بثبوت البقاء في الممكنات ونفيه عنه تعالى وبه قال الكعبي وأتباعه . قوله البقاء صفة تقتضى ترجيح الوجود على العدم فقال له الموجود الذى لا يبقى له بدله أيضاً ما يقتضى ترجح وجوده على عدمه فإذا هذا الحكم ليس ما يختص بالبقاء ، إلا أن يكون الترجيح إلى الزمان الثاني ، والتحقيق فيه أن البقاء مقارنة الوجود لاكثر من زمان واحد بعد الزمان الأول وذلك لا يعقل فما لا يكون زمانيا ، واعتبر الحكم بكون الكل أعظم من جزئه فإنه لا يمكن أن يقال إنه واقع في زمان أو في جميع الأزمنة كما لا يقال إنه واقع في مكان أو في جميع الأمكنة، وإذا كان الحكم كذلك فما يتوقف عليه الحكم كالتصورات أولى بأن يكون كذلك ، وعلة الزمان لا يكون زمانيا فكيف مبدأ الكل ، فإذا اتصافه بالبقاء نوع من التشبيه بالزمانيات ، وأما كون البقاء باقيا أو غير باق وإن كان باقيا فبقاؤه إما بذاته أو بغيره فحكمه حكم الأمور الاعتبارية التي توجد في العقل فقط وتنقطع عند عدم الاعتبار . وقوله : وأما في الشاهد فليس بمعنى أيضاً إشارة إلى إبطال مذهب الكمى . وقوله : يلى الحدوث ليس صفة زائدة لجوابه ما مر ثم إن كان الحدوث، نفس الحصول في الزمان الأول فالبقاء حصول في زمان مشروط بحصول فى زمان قبله وإلا لم يمكن زمانه ثابتا ، والحصول في الزمان الأول ليس مشروطا بالحصول فى زمان آخر فالاختلاف بينهما بوجود هذا الشرط و من هذا الاعتبار يتحقق ما قلنا وهو أن البقاء مقارنة الوجود لأكثر من زمان واحد بعد الزمان الأول . وعدمه فقط
(1) I say: And this is another position: it affirms permanence in contingents and denies it of Him, exalted, and al-Ka'bi and his followers held this. His statement: 'permanence is an attribute that necessitates the preference of existence over non-existence' – so one says: the existent that does not have permanence also has what necessitates the preponderance of its existence over non-existence, so this judgment is not specific to permanence, unless the preponderance is to the second time. The verification of it is that permanence is the conjunction of existence for more than one time after the first time, and that is not conceivable for what is not temporal. Consider the judgment that the whole is greater than its part: it cannot be said that it occurs in time or in all times, just as it cannot be said that it occurs in place or in all places. And when the judgment is like that, then what the judgment depends on, like concepts, is even more so. And the cause of time is not temporal, so how about the Principle of all? So His being described with permanence is a kind of anthropomorphism (tashbih) with temporal things. As for whether permanence is permanent or not, and if it is permanent, its permanence is either by itself or by another; its ruling is the ruling of mental considerations that exist only in the mind and cease when the consideration ceases. And his statement: 'As for in the witnessed world, it is not a meaning either' is an indication of refuting the position of al-Ka'bi. And his statement: 'following hadath, it is not an additional attribute' – I refer to the previous answer. Then, if hadath is just occurrence in the first time, then permanence is occurrence in a time conditioned by occurrence in a time before it, otherwise its time would not be established. And occurrence in the first time is not conditioned by occurrence in another time; the difference between them is by the existence of this condition. And from this consideration, what we said is verified: that permanence is the conjunction of existence for more than one time after the first time. And its non-existence only.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This is a lengthy marginal commentary (by a later scholar) analyzing the baqa' debate. The commentator first notes a third position: that baqa' is affirmed for contingents but denied for God, held by al-Ka'bi. Then he examines the argument that baqa' is an attribute that tips the scale of existence over non-existence, and notes that even non-permanent things have some ground for existence, so it's not unique to baqa' unless it refers to duration. He then argues that baqa' essentially means existence for more than one time, which is meaningless for a timeless being. He draws an analogy with the judgment that the whole is greater than the part, which is not temporal. Applying temporal concepts to God is a form of anthropomorphism. He then discusses whether baqa' itself would need to be permanent, leading to a regress, and says it is a mental consideration only. Finally, he connects the earlier statement about hadath to conclude that baqa' is merely existence after a previous time, not an attribute.

Key Terms
أمور اعتبارية (mental considerations)
علة الزمان (cause of time)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqa' is a real, subsistent attribute that can be predicated of God.
Correction: Baqa' is a mental consideration; it is merely the concept of existence after a prior time, not a real attribute.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator classify baqa' after considering the dilemma of its permanence?
A) A real attribute of God
B) A mental consideration
C) A synonym for hadath
D) An impossible concept

Key Takeaway
Baqa' is not a real attribute but a mental consideration, defined as existence after a prior time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attribute of permanence (baqa'), do you tend to imagine it as a 'thing' added to His essence, like a quality you can point to? How does realizing it is only a mental consideration change the way you relate to God's eternity? What shift in your heart does this demand?
?
If baqa' is merely a mental tool to express that God exists without end, then your concept of God's eternity is not about adding something to Him but about affirming His continued existence. Are there other attributes you may be mistakenly treating as separate realities instead of seeing them as aspects of His single, simple essence? How can you train your mind to avoid this error in your worship and reflection?

Proof That God Knows All Things
Razi states the Muslim position and gives a proof based on God's life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument for God's omniscience and why limited knowledge would require a discriminator.

Core Idea
God's life entails universal knowledge without need for a discriminator.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a concise proof for God's omniscience. He argues that since God is living, He is capable of knowing all things; restricting His knowledge would require an impossible specifying factor. This argument refutes both philosophers and certain Muslim sects who limit divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Focus on the logical structure: premise (God is living) leads to capability of all knowledge, then if restricted, need for mukhassis.
2
Identify the mukhassis argument
Understand that if God knew only some things, there must be a reason (mukhassis) for that specification. Since God is self-sufficient, such a discriminator is impossible.
This is a standard kalam argument against limitation.
3
Consider the opponents
Note that the philosophers (who limit knowledge to universals) and a group from the Muslim community are opposed. Reflect on why these views are problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : مذهب أكثر المسلمين أنه تعالى عالم بكل المعلومات خلافا للفلاسفة ولقوم من أهل الملة لنا أنه تعالى لكونه حيا يصح أن يكون عالما بكل المعلومات لو احتص عالميته بالبعض دون البعض لافتقر إلى مخصص وهو محال و من الدهرية من زعم أنه لا يعلم ذاته لأن العلم أمر إضافي فلو علم ذاته لسكانت ذاته مضافة إلى نفسه وإضافة الشيء إلى نفسه محال
Issue: The position of most Muslims is that He, exalted, knows all known things (ma'lumat), contrary to the philosophers and a group from the people of the religion. Our proof: He, exalted, by virtue of being living (hayy) can be knower of all known things. If His knowing were confined to some things to the exclusion of others, it would need a discriminator (mukhassis), and that is impossible. And among the Dahriyya [materialists] is one who claimed that He does not know His own essence, because knowledge is a relational matter (amr idafi); if He knew His essence, His essence would be related to itself, and the relation of a thing to itself is impossible.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the discussion on divine knowledge. Razi states that the majority of Muslims hold that God knows all knowable things, opposing the philosophers who limit God's knowledge to universals, and a group from the Muslim community (possibly the Jahmiyya or others). The proof is that God is alive (hayy), which qualifies Him to be a knower of all things. If His knowledge were restricted to some things, there would be need for a specifying factor (mukhassis), which is absurd for God. Then he mentions an objection from the Dahriyya (materialists) who deny that God knows Himself, based on the idea that knowledge is a relational attribute implying a relation between subject and object, and a thing cannot be related to itself. This sets up the need to refute that objection.

Key Terms
معلومات (known things)
حي (living)
مخصص (discriminator/specifier)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge can be limited to certain things, e.g., only universals.
Correction: Razi shows that any limitation would require a specifying factor external to God, which is impossible; therefore God must know all things.

Checkpoint
What would be required if God's knowledge were limited to some things?
A) A volitional act
B) A discriminator (mukhassis)
C) A prior cause
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
God's life implies universal knowledge; any restriction would need an impossible discriminator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God knows every single thought you have, even the ones you hide from yourself? What would change in your life if you lived with that certainty?
?
If you believed that God's knowledge were limited, how would that affect your fear of Him? How does the fact that He knows everything you do, even in secret, make you feel right now?
?
The proof says any limitation would need a discriminator. Do you sometimes act as if God's knowledge is limited, as if He doesn't see certain areas of your life? What area are you most hesitant to bring before His all-encompassing knowledge?

The Objection That God Cannot Know Himself
Razi quotes the Dahriyya objection against God's self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Dahriyya objection that God cannot know Himself because knowledge is relational.

Core Idea
If knowledge is a relation, then self-knowledge would be a relation of a thing to itself, which is impossible.

In this passage, Razi presents a specific objection from the Dahriyya (materialists) against divine self-knowledge. They argue that knowledge is a relational attribute (amr idafi), and a relation of a thing to itself is impossible. This objection sets up the need for a deeper analysis of the nature of divine knowledge and relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the part of the passage starting from 'And among the Dahriyya...' Understand the claim: God does not know His own essence because knowledge is relational.
2
Understand the concept of 'idafa'
Relational attributes (idafa) require two distinct relata. The Dahriyya argue that if God knows Himself, His essence would be both subject and object, implying relation to itself, which is absurd.
This is a philosophical objection.
3
Prepare for the refutation
Note that Razi only presents the objection here; the refutation will follow in the next part. Reflect on possible responses: can a thing be related to itself in some sense? This leads to deeper theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : مذهب أكثر المسلمين أنه تعالى عالم بكل المعلومات خلافا للفلاسفة ولقوم من أهل الملة لنا أنه تعالى لكونه حيا يصح أن يكون عالما بكل المعلومات لو احتص عالميته بالبعض دون البعض لافتقر إلى مخصص وهو محال و من الدهرية من زعم أنه لا يعلم ذاته لأن العلم أمر إضافي فلو علم ذاته لسكانت ذاته مضافة إلى نفسه وإضافة الشيء إلى نفسه محال
Issue: The position of most Muslims is that He, exalted, knows all known things (ma'lumat), contrary to the philosophers and a group from the people of the religion. Our proof: He, exalted, by virtue of being living (hayy) can be knower of all known things. If His knowing were confined to some things to the exclusion of others, it would need a discriminator (mukhassis), and that is impossible. And among the Dahriyya [materialists] is one who claimed that He does not know His own essence, because knowledge is a relational matter (amr idafi); if He knew His essence, His essence would be related to itself, and the relation of a thing to itself is impossible.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the discussion on divine knowledge. Razi states that the majority of Muslims hold that God knows all knowable things, opposing the philosophers who limit God's knowledge to universals, and a group from the Muslim community (possibly the Jahmiyya or others). The proof is that God is alive (hayy), which qualifies Him to be a knower of all things. If His knowledge were restricted to some things, there would be need for a specifying factor (mukhassis), which is absurd for God. Then he mentions an objection from the Dahriyya (materialists) who deny that God knows Himself, based on the idea that knowledge is a relational attribute implying a relation between subject and object, and a thing cannot be related to itself. This sets up the need to refute that objection.

Key Terms
علم إضافي (relational knowledge)
إضافة (relation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that self-knowledge is impossible because it implies a relation of a thing to itself.
Correction: While this might seem logical, Islamic theology holds that God's knowledge is not a relational attribute in the usual sense, and His simplicity allows for self-knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the Dahriyya objection, why cannot God know Himself?
A) Because knowledge is limited to finite beings
B) Because knowledge is relational and a thing cannot be related to itself
C) Because God is immutable
D) Because God is not a material being

Key Takeaway
The Dahriyya objection challenges self-knowledge based on the nature of relational attributes, requiring a nuanced theological response.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself doubting that God knows you fully, perhaps because you think He is too transcendent to be concerned with details? How does the Dahriyya objection—that self-knowledge is impossible for God—challenge or reinforce that doubt? Will you let such logical puzzles shake your trust, or will you seek to understand the limits of your own reasoning?
?
When you encounter an objection against a core belief, like this one against God's self-knowledge, do you engage with it or dismiss it? This lesson shows that the objection has a structure that must be examined. Are you willing to examine the premises of your own doubts and submit them to the deeper wisdom of revelation? Commit now to investigate one doubt you have with the same methodical care.

Permanence as an Additional Attribute: Objection and Answer
Razi presents objection and answer on baqa' as an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that permanence (baqa') is not an additional attribute, based on the parity argument with origination (huduth).

Core Idea
Permanence is not an additional attribute; it is a relational concept like origination.

This passage presents a classic kalam debate: Is permanence a separate attribute added to God's essence? The objectors say yes, because the essence becomes permanent after not being permanent. But Razi provides a powerful counter-argument using parity: by the same logic, origination would also have to be an additional attribute, which is absurd. This lesson unpacks that argument and the deeper discussion that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the flow of objection and answer come through.
Notice the use of 'احتجوا' and 'والجواب'.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The objectors argue permanence must be additional because the essence becomes permanent. Find the answer that uses parity with origination.
The answer also shows the impossibility of origination being additional.
3
Understand the parity argument
If permanence is additional because the essence changed from non-permanent to permanent, then by the same reasoning, origination would be additional because the essence changed from non-originated to originated. But origination cannot be additional, so permanence cannot be either.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the dialectical exchange
An opponent clarifies that origination is simply occurrence in the first time. The response says permanence is similarly occurrence in the second time.
This sets up the discussion that follows.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi's note explains that permanence is a relational concept, not a real attribute, and attributing it to God is a form of anthropomorphism.
Consider why this argument matters for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا بأن الذات لم تكن باقية حال الحدوث ثم صارت باقية فوجب أن يكون البقاء زائداً . والجواب : بأنه معارض بأن الذات كانت حادثة زمان الحدوث ثم حال البقاء ما بقيت حادثة فيلزم أن يكون الحدوث صفة زائدة وهو محال على ما تقدم . فإن قلت : الحدوث نفس حصوله في الزمان الأول. قلت : البقاء نفس حصوله في الزمان الثانى. (١) أقول : وههذا مذهب آخر وهو يقول بثبوت البقاء في الممكنات ونفيه عنه تعالى وبه قال الكعبي وأتباعه . قوله البقاء صفة تقتضى ترجيح الوجود على العدم فقال له الموجود الذى لا يبقى له بدله أيضاً ما يقتضى ترجح وجوده على عدمه فإذا هذا الحكم ليس ما يختص بالبقاء ، إلا أن يكون الترجيح إلى الزمان الثاني ، والتحقيق فيه أن البقاء مقارنة الوجود لاكثر من زمان واحد بعد الزمان الأول وذلك لا يعقل فما لا يكون زمانيا ، واعتبر الحكم بكون الكل أعظم من جزئه فإنه لا يمكن أن يقال إنه واقع في زمان أو في جميع الأزمنة كما لا يقال إنه واقع في مكان أو في جميع الأمكنة، وإذا كان الحكم كذلك فما يتوقف عليه الحكم كالتصورات أولى بأن يكون كذلك ، وعلة الزمان لا يكون زمانيا فكيف مبدأ الكل ، فإذا اتصافه بالبقاء نوع من التشبيه بالزمانيات ، وأما كون البقاء باقيا أو غير باق وإن كان باقيا فبقاؤه إما بذاته أو بغيره فحكمه حكم الأمور الاعتبارية التي توجد في العقل فقط وتنقطع عند عدم الاعتبار . وقوله : وأما في الشاهد فليس بمعنى أيضاً إشارة إلى إبطال مذهب الكمى . وقوله : يلى الحدوث ليس صفة زائدة لجوابه ما مر ثم إن كان الحدوث، نفس الحصول في الزمان الأول فالبقاء حصول في زمان مشروط بحصول فى زمان قبله وإلا لم يمكن زمانه ثابتا ، والحصول في الزمان الأول ليس مشروطا بالحصول فى زمان آخر فالاختلاف بينهما بوجود هذا الشرط و من هذا الاعتبار يتحقق ما قلنا وهو أن البقاء مقارنة الوجود لأكثر من زمان واحد بعد الزمان الأول . وعدمه فقط
They argued: The essence was not permanent at the time of origination, then it became permanent, so permanence must be additional. The answer: It is countered that the essence was originated at the time of origination, then at the time of permanence it was not originated, so origination would have to be an additional attribute, which is impossible as previously established. If you say: Origination is the same as its occurring in the first time. I say: Permanence is the same as its occurring in the second time. (1) I say: This is another doctrine: it affirms permanence in contingent things and negates it of God Almighty; al-Ka'bi and his followers held this. He says: Permanence is an attribute that requires preference of existence over non-existence. So it is said to him: The existent that does not persist also has something that requires preference of its existence over its non-existence, so this judgment is not specific to permanence, unless the preference is toward the second time. The verification is that permanence is the coexistence of existence for more than one time after the first time, and that is not conceivable for what is not temporal. Consider the judgment that the whole is greater than its part: it cannot be said that it occurs in time or in all times, just as it cannot be said that it occurs in place or in all places. If the judgment is like that, then what the judgment depends on, like concepts, is more fitting to be like that. The cause of time is not temporal, so how about the origin of all things? Thus, His being described by permanence is a kind of anthropomorphism with temporal things. As for whether permanence is enduring or not, and if enduring, its endurance is either by itself or by another, its ruling is like the ruling of mental considerations that exist only in the mind and cease when consideration ceases. His saying: 'And as for the witnessed, it is not a meaning either' is an indication of refuting the doctrine of al-Kamī. His saying: 'Following origination is not an additional attribute' in answer to what has passed. Then if origination is the occurrence in the first time, then permanence is occurrence in a time conditioned by occurrence in a time before it, otherwise its time could not be fixed. Occurrence in the first time is not conditioned by occurrence in another time, so the difference between them is the existence of this condition. From this consideration, what we said is verified: that permanence is the coexistence of existence for more than one time after the first time. And its non-existence only.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding whether permanence (baqa') is an additional attribute to essence. The objectors argue that since an essence becomes permanent after being non-permanent, permanence must be an additional attribute. The answer uses parity: origination (huduth) also occurs at the time of origination, but at the time of permanence the essence is not originated, so by the same logic origination would have to be an additional attribute, which is impossible. Then an opponent clarifies that origination is simply occurrence in the first time, and the response says permanence is simply occurrence in the second time. The author's marginal note (1) then expands: this is the doctrine of al-Ka'bi who affirms permanence for contingents but denies it for God. He criticizes the notion that permanence is an attribute that gives preference, arguing that all existents have something giving preference, so that judgment is not specific. He then defines permanence as coexistence of existence for more than one time, which is inconceivable for non-temporal beings. Applying the analogy of 'the whole is greater than the part'—a judgment not located in time—shows that what depends on such judgments is also non-temporal. Thus attributing permanence to God is a kind of anthropomorphism. The note concludes by distinguishing between origination (unconditioned) and permanence (conditioned by prior occurrence), agreeing that permanence is not an additional attribute but a relational consideration.

Key Terms
البقاء
الحدوث
الصفة الزائدة
الكعبي
المقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Permanence is a positive attribute added to God's essence, just like knowledge or power.
Correction: The passage argues that permanence is a relational concept (coexistence with multiple times), not a real attribute. Attributing it to God implies anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why is permanence not an additional attribute?
A) Because it is identical to existence.
B) Because if it were, then origination would also have to be an additional attribute, which is impossible.
C) Because permanence is only in contingent things.
D) Because the objectors' argument is based on a false premise.

Key Takeaway
Permanence is not an additional attribute; it is a relational concept that applies only to temporal beings, and attributing it to God implies anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah as 'permanent' or 'everlasting', do you unconsciously imagine a being that persists through time? How does this lesson challenge that image, and what does it demand you change in your conception of Him?
?
If describing God with permanence is a form of anthropomorphism, how careful are you in your own speech about Allah? Are there other attributes you might be using that borrow from temporal categories without realizing it?
?
The passage emphasizes that the cause of time is not temporal. How does this truth affect your worship? Does your worship reflect a God who is beyond time, or do you treat Him as if He is bound by your schedule and deadlines?
